reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    A model that performs a reverse cumulative sum operation along a specified dimension.

    Parameters:
        dim (int): The dimension along which to perform the reverse cumulative sum.
    """

    def __init__(self, dim):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.dim = dim

    def forward(self, x):
        return torch.cumsum(x.flip(self.dim), dim=self.dim).flip(self.dim)

batch_size = 32768
input_shape = (32768,)
dim = 1

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, *input_shape)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [dim]

reasoning
The reference implementation does a reverse cumulative sum along a specified dimension. Specifically: 1. `x.flip(dim)` 2. `torch.cumsum(..., dim=dim)` 3. `.flip(dim)` For the given shapes: `batch_size=32768`, `input_shape=(32768,)`, `dim=1`. So `x` is shape `[32768, 32768]`, and we're doing a reverse cumsum along dimension 1 (the inner dimension of size 32768). This is equivalent to computing for each row `i`: `out[i, j] = sum_{k=j}^{N-1} x[i, k]` where `N=32768`. We need to write a custom CUDA kernel for this. First, let's think about memory access patterns and parallelism. A straightforward approach: - Each row can be processed independently. - Within each row, we need to compute a suffix sum. - We can use a block per row, and do a parallel scan within the block. - Since N=32768 threads per row might be too many (though we can use 1024 threads and loop), we need an efficient algorithm. But wait, the inner dimension is 32768. If we use one block per row: - If block size = 1024, each thread handles 32 elements. - We can load elements into shared memory, do a tree-based scan, or a sequential scan per thread then scan across threads. Actually, for a suffix sum, we can do: 1. Load a chunk of the row into shared memory (or registers). 2. Compute the suffix sum. Alternatively, we can view this as: `out[i, j] = total_sum[i] - prefix_sum[i, j-1]`, where `total_sum[i]` is the sum of the entire row, and `prefix_sum` is the standard prefix sum. But computing prefix sum then subtracting from total might be similar complexity. Wait: `torch.cumsum(x.flip(dim), dim=dim).flip(dim)` is indeed the suffix sum. But note: `out[i, j] = x[i, j] + x[i, j+1] + ... + x[i, N-1]`. This can also be written as `out = torch.cumsum(x, dim=dim).flip(dim)`? No, that's not right. `cumsum(x.flip(dim)).flip(dim)` means: Let `y[i, j] = x[i, N-1-j]`. Then `z[i, j] = sum_{k=0}^j y[i, k] = sum_{k=0}^j x[i, N-1-k] = sum_{k=N-1-j}^{N-1} x[i, k]`. Then `out[i, j] = z[i, N-1-j] = sum_{k=j}^{N-1} x[i, k]`. Yes, suffix sum. Alternative: `out = x.flip(dim).cumsum(dim).flip(dim)`. Now, for performance on a large 2D tensor [32768, 32768], which is quite big (1B elements, 4GB). The PyTorch implementation likely does: 1. One kernel for flip (out-of-place memory copy with index reversal) 2. One kernel for cumsum (likely using a scan algorithm) 3. Another kernel for flip Wait, does PyTorch fuse these? Let's think. `x.flip(dim)` returns a view? No, in recent PyTorch, `flip` returns a copy (it calls a kernel). Actually, `torch.flip` does not return a view; it copies data. So: - Step 1: allocate temp1, kernel to copy reversed. - Step 2: allocate temp2, kernel to compute cumsum on temp1. - Step 3: allocate output, kernel to copy reversed. So there are 3 kernels and 2 intermediate allocations. A custom kernel can do this in a single pass, or at least fewer passes, and avoid extra memory allocations. Even better: we can compute suffix sum directly without flipping. Algorithm: For each row, compute the suffix sum. Since the tensor is large, we want to maximize memory bandwidth. The operation is memory-bound (each element is read once and written once, with some adds). Actually, if we do a single pass per row serially within a thread: - Read row from right to left, keep running sum, write output. This is O(N) work per row, and each element is read once and written once. Memory traffic: read 4GB, write 4GB. Total 8GB. Peak memory bandwidth of an A100 is ~2TB/s. So the absolute lower bound is about 4ms. (8GB / 2TB/s = 4ms). H100 is higher. But wait, with coalescing: if threads in a warp access consecutive memory locations, we get good bandwidth. If each thread handles one row, and threads in a block process consecutive rows: - Row `i` is at offset `i * N`. So thread `bid` accesses `bid * N + tid`... but if one thread per row, consecutive threads access base addresses separated by `N` elements = 32768*4 = 131KB. That's way more than a cache line, so memory accesses within a warp are not coalesced. This would be terrible. We should have threads within a warp cooperate on the same row or consecutive columns. If we use a block per row, with threads processing consecutive columns: - Thread `tid` in block `bid` processes column `tid` of row `bid`. But then how do they compute suffix sum? They need to communicate via shared memory. Parallel scan approach: - Block size = 256 or 512 or 1024. - For row length N=32768, block size 1024 means each thread handles 32 elements. - Load data into shared memory (or registers). - Compute local suffix sums per thread. - Then do an inter-thread scan to combine. - Finally, write back. But a simpler approach: since the inner dimension is 32768, we could also have each warp (32 threads) handle a row? No, 32 threads for 32768 elements means 1024 elements per thread. Too much work imbalance? Actually, it's fine if each thread serially sums a chunk. But again, coalescing: if thread 0 reads columns 0..1023, thread 1 reads 1024..2047, etc., memory accesses within a warp are not coalesced because threads in a warp access different segments far apart. Wait: warp reads 32 consecutive words from memory at a time. If thread 0 accesses addr 0, thread 1 accesses addr 1024, ... these are strided. Not coalesced. To coalesce, thread `tid` in a warp should access column `base + tid`. So for a block of `T` threads working on one row: - Iteration 0: threads 0..T-1 read columns 0..T-1. - Iteration 1: read columns T..2T-1. - etc. This is coalesced. So for a serial suffix sum per block: we can have one block per row, and the block processes the row from left to right or right to left? For suffix sum, it's easier to process left to right if we do prefix of reversed, but that's flip again. Let's just process right to left to compute suffix sum directly. - Each block handles one row. - Threads in block (say 256) read a segment of the row from right to left? Actually, reading consecutively from memory is better. - We want the output at column j to be sum_{k=j}^{N-1} x[i,k]. We can process from right to left: start at column N-1, move to 0. - Thread 0 handles column N-1, thread 1 handles N-2? That would reverse order and not coalesce well unless we read in blocks. Better: process the row in chunks from right to left, but within a chunk, threads access consecutive columns. Wait, if we read chunk starting at `N-256`, columns `N-256` to `N-1`, then thread `t` accesses column `N-256 + t`. This is coalesced. Then next chunk `N-512` to `N-257`, etc. In each chunk, we need to know the suffix sum of everything to the right. So we process chunks from right to left. Algorithm for block per row: 1. Initialize a running sum `carry = 0` (in shared memory). 2. For `chunk_start` from `N - blockDim.x` down to `0` in steps of `-blockDim.x`: a. Load `x[i, chunk_start + tid]` into shared memory `shmem[tid]`. b. __syncthreads. c. Compute suffix sum of `shmem` in parallel using a standard scan/broadcast. Actually, we need suffix sums of the chunk: for element at offset `t` within chunk (where chunk covers columns `chunk_start .. chunk_start+blockDim.x-1`), the suffix within the chunk is `sum_{k=t}^{blockDim.x-1} shmem[k]`. d. Then add `carry` to each element. e. Write back `output[i, chunk_start + tid]`. f. Update `carry += sum of entire chunk` (which is the prefix sum of reversed or just total of chunk, or equivalently carry after processing leftmost element? Let's see: after step c, the suffix sum at index `chunk_start` is `carry + sum(shmem[0..blockDim.x-1])`. So the new `carry` for the next chunk to the left should be `carry + sum(shmem[0..blockDim.x-1])`. 3. Done. But doing a parallel suffix scan on shared memory per chunk involves log(blockDim.x) steps with syncthreads. For 32 chunks (since N/blockDim.x = 128 for blockDim=256), that's 128 * log2(256) = 128*8 = 1024 steps. That's okay. However, can we do better? Since the arithmetic intensity is low, memory bandwidth is the bottleneck. The parallel scan overhead might be low compared to memory time. But we can also do a "sequential per thread" approach with better coalescing. Another idea: Let's just do a very simple kernel where each thread handles one row, but we read/write via vectorized loads/stores (e.g., float4) if aligned. While this is not fully coalesced across threads in a warp (since each thread is in a different row), modern GPUs handle coalescing for strided accesses to some extent via L2 cache. Wait, if rows are 131KB apart, consecutive threads in a warp hit different cache lines. Each warp memory instruction would generate 32 cache lines, which is terrible. So thread-per-row is bad. What if we transpose the problem? We can launch a grid of [N / 32, N?]. Let's think. Actually, for row-wise operations, block-per-row is standard and gives good coalescing if threads handle consecutive columns. Given N=32768, blockDim.x can be 256, 512, or 1024. Let's choose 512 or 1024. 32768 / 1024 = 32 chunks per row. Perfect. So one block per row, 1024 threads. Each thread loads one element, we do a parallel suffix scan on 1024 elements in shared mem, write back. This is clean and should be efficient. Let's design the parallel suffix scan in shared memory. Standard inclusive scan then adjust? Or exclusive scan then adjust? For suffix sum: out[j] = sum_{k=j}^{M-1} in[k]. This is equivalent to reverse(inclusive_scan(reverse(in))). Or we can do: first compute total sum. Then do an exclusive scan, subtract from total. But exclusive scan is also log steps. Actually, a simple Blelloch-style exclusive prefix sum in forward direction, then subtract from total. Wait, we want suffix sums. Let `M = blockDim.x`. If we compute the prefix sum on the reversed array in shared memory: `shmem_rev[t] = shmem[M-1-t]`. `prefix_rev[t] = sum_{k=0}^t shmem_rev[k] = sum_{k=M-1-t}^{M-1} shmem[k]`. Then `out[t] = prefix_rev[M-1-t]`. Alternatively, just compute prefix sum on `shmem` in-place (forward), then `out[t] = total - prefix[t-1]` (with prefix[-1]=0). That requires a barrier and then each thread computes its value. But doing an in-place Blelloch exclusive prefix sum is经典. Steps: 1. Load into shared memory `sdata[tid]`. 2. Up-sweep (reduction) tree. 3. Set last element to 0. 4. Down-sweep tree. After this, `sdata[tid]` contains `sum_{k=0}^{tid-1} shmem_original[k]`. Then total sum is saved before clearing, or can be computed as `sdata[M-1] + shmem_original[M-1]`. Actually after up-sweep, `sdata[M-1]` is total sum. We can save it in a variable `total` before setting to 0. Then `suffix[tid] = total - sdata[tid]`. Wait: `sdata[tid]` is exclusive prefix sum of original up to `tid-1`. So `sum_{k=tid}^{M-1} original[k] = total - sum_{k=0}^{tid-1} original[k] = total - sdata[tid]`. Yes. So: - After exclusive prefix sum, `suffix = total - sdata[tid]`. - Then `suffix += carry`. - Write back. Wait, is that correct? Let's check tid=0: `sdata[0] = 0`, so `suffix[0] = total - 0 = total`. Correct. tid=M-1: `sdata[M-1] = sum_{0}^{M-2}`, so `suffix[M-1] = total - sum_{0}^{M-2} = original[M-1]`. Correct. So we need an exclusive prefix sum implementation. But note: we need `total` to update `carry`. `carry` should become `carry + total`. However, there's a subtlety: `total` is the sum of the current chunk. In the previous chunk (to the right), `carry` was the sum of everything to its right. For the next chunk to the left, `carry_new = carry_old + total`? No. Let's label chunks from right to left. Chunk 0: columns [N-M, N-1]. `carry_before` = 0. `suffix[j] = carry_before + suffix_within[j]` where `j` from 0..M-1 mapping to cols N-M+j. `carry_after` = `carry_before + total_chunk0` = total of chunk 0. Chunk 1: columns [N-2M, N-M-1]. `carry_before` = `carry_after` = total of chunk 0. `suffix[j] = carry_before + suffix_within[j]`. `carry_after` = `carry_before + total_chunk1`. So yes, `carry_next = carry_prev + total`. We can process chunks from right to left in a for loop within the block. All threads participate. Implementation plan: ```cuda __global__ void reverse_cumsum_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int rows, int cols) { extern __shared__ float sdata[]; // size = blockDim.x * sizeof(float) // Actually, if blockDim.x=1024, we need sdata[1024], plus maybe a second array for scan? // We can do in-place Blelloch scan in sdata. int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= rows) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int blockSize = blockDim.x; const float* row_in = input + row * cols; float* row_out = output + row * cols; float carry = 0.0f; // Process chunks from right to left for (int chunk_start = cols - blockSize; chunk_start >= 0; chunk_start -= blockSize) { int col = chunk_start + tid; // Load float val = row_in[col]; sdata[tid] = val; __syncthreads(); // Up-sweep (exclusive prefix sum preparation) // We need to preserve original? For tid extraction: we can load val into register. // After exclusive scan, sdata[tid] will hold sum[0..tid-1]. // We need total = sdata[blockSize-1] + val (where val is original last element). // But wait, in-place exclusive scan on sdata overwrites values. // Up-sweep phase: for (int d = 1; d < blockSize; d *= 2) { if (tid < blockSize / (2*d)) { int ai = (2*tid+1)*d - 1; int bi = (2*tid+2)*d - 1; sdata[bi] += sdata[ai]; } __syncthreads(); } if (tid == 0) { // Save total and clear last element // But we need total. Let's have a shared total. // Or: total = sdata[blockSize-1]; sdata[blockSize-1] = 0; } __syncthreads(); // Down-sweep for (int d = blockSize/2; d >= 1; d /= 2) { if (tid < blockSize / (2*d)) { int ai = (2*tid+1)*d - 1; int bi = (2*tid+2)*d - 1; float t = sdata[ai]; sdata[ai] = sdata[bi]; sdata[bi] += t; } __syncthreads(); } // Now sdata[tid] = exclusive prefix sum of original vals. // Total = sdata[blockSize-1] + val_original_of_last. // suffix = total - sdata[tid] // output = suffix + carry // Hmm, but val_original_of_last is in register of tid=blockSize-1. // We can broadcast total via shared memory. float exclusive = sdata[tid]; float my_val = val; // saved register __syncthreads(); // ensure we're done reading sdata before overwriting // Compute total. Only tid=blockSize-1 needs to compute? // Actually any thread can: total = sdata[blockSize-1] + last_val. // But we need last_val. We can store it in shared mem before down-sweep. // Simpler: before up-sweep, copy original to another shared mem bank? Or just do a separate reduction for total. // Actually, total is just the sum of all vals in the block. We can compute it with a warp shuffle or just a simple shared memory reduction. // But we already have the sum after up-sweep in sdata[blockSize-1]. The issue is we overwrote it with 0 for down-sweep. // So let's save the total into a separate shared variable `total_s` before clearing. } } ``` This is getting a bit messy with shared memory. We can use two shared memory arrays: `temp` and `original`, but that doubles smem. For 1024 floats, that's 4KB, which is fine. Alternatively, a simpler approach for suffix scan on a chunk: use a serial scan per thread but transpose to warps for coalescing? Let's reconsider. Another possibility: Use `std::reverse`? No. We can use a much simpler algorithm: load a tile of `TILE` elements into shared memory, compute the suffix sums serially by one thread? No, we want all threads to write. But if we use one thread per element, we need scan. What about using warp-level primitives? We can compute within-warp suffix sums using `__shfl_up_sync` or `__shfl_down_sync`. For warp size 32: `suffix = val;` `for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float n = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, suffix, offset); if (lane + offset < 32) suffix += n; }` Wait, that's prefix sum (to the right). Let's be careful. `__shfl_down_sync(mask, var, delta)` gets value from thread `lane+delta`. For suffix sum: `out[lane] = sum_{k=lane}^{31} in[k]`. So each lane needs sum of all lanes to its right. `sum = val;` `for (offset=1; offset<32; offset*=2) { float n = __shfl_down_sync(mask, sum, offset); if (lane_id + offset < 32) sum += n; }` Yes, this computes inclusive suffix sum within the warp. Then for inter-warp, we need to combine. This is an efficient strategy. Let's design a kernel using warp shuffle for intra-warp scan and shared memory for inter-warp. Block size: 1024 threads = 32 warps. Load a chunk of 1024 elements into registers (each thread loads one). Compute inclusive suffix sum within warp: ```cuda float val = row_in[col]; float sum = val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); if (lane_id + offset < 32) sum += other; } ``` Now `sum` for thread `lane` is warp_suffix[lane] = sum of original vals in warp for lanes >= lane. Let `warp_id = tid / 32`, `lane_id = tid % 32`. Now we need the suffix sum across warps. For a given warp `w`, we need `warp_carry[w] = sum of all elements in warps > w` within this block. Wait: block processes columns `chunk_start .. chunk_start+1023`. Warps within block handle consecutive 32-element sub-chunks. Warp 0 handles columns `chunk_start + 0..31`. Warp 1 handles `chunk_start + 32..63`. ... Warp 31 handles `chunk_start + 992..1023`. We want suffix sums. For an element in warp `w`, the total suffix = sum within warp from its lane to end + sum of all warps after `w`. So we need `warp_suffix_total[w] = sum of all elements in warps w, w+1, ..., 31`. Equivalently, compute suffix sum of `warp_total[w]` (where `warp_total[w]` is sum of all elements in warp w). So steps for one chunk: 1. `col = chunk_start + tid`. 2. `val = row_in[col];` 3. `intra = val;` compute inclusive suffix within warp using shfl_down. 4. `warp_total = __reduce_add_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val);` // sum of original vals in warp. After writing, update `carry` for next iteration. `carry += shared[0];` because `shared[0]` after step 7 is `sum_{k=0}^{31} warp_total[k] = sum of all elements in this chunk`. But we need to make sure all threads read the same value. That's fine, it's a broadcast. Wait, which thread updates `carry`? Actually `carry` is in a register local to each thread. To avoid divergence and for correctness, each thread can keep its own `carry` register initialized to 0, and after each chunk add `shared[0]` to it. But `shared[0]` after step 7 is `inclusive_suffix[0]` = total sum of all warps = total chunk sum. Yes. So `carry += shared[0];` update per thread. All threads in the block have identical `carry` values throughout. Wait, we need to be careful: after step 7, `shared[warp_id]` is overwritten with inclusive suffix. So `shared[0]` is total chunk sum. Since all threads read `shared[0]`, no problem. They all update their own `carry` register identically. But hold on: in step 7, the warp that does the suffix scan uses `shared` as input. Then writes back. If all warps are participating in `__syncthreads()` before step 9, it's fine. Potential issue: shared memory bank conflicts. `shared[warp_id]` accesses by all threads in a warp go to the same bank? `warp_id = tid / 32`. So threads 0..31 have warp_id=0, so they all access bank 0? No, only lane 0 writes warp_total. Others don't access shared in step 5 except in step 7 where warp 0 accesses `shared[lane_id]`. That's consecutive, no bank conflict. In step 9, all threads read `shared[warp_id+1]`. Threads in the same warp have the same `warp_id`, so they all read the same address. So the kernel is: ```cuda __global__ void reverse_cumsum_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int rows, int cols) { __shared__ float shared[33]; // 32 warps + 1 padding for shared[32]=0 maybe. // Actually need 32 for warp totals, and maybe 1 extra for carry, but we can use register carry. int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= rows) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane_id = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; const float* row_in = input + (int64_t)row * cols; float* row_out = output + (int64_t)row * cols; float carry = 0.0f; for (int chunk_start = cols - blockDim.x; chunk_start >= 0; chunk_start -= blockDim.x) { int col = chunk_start + tid; float val = row_in[col]; // Intra-warp inclusive suffix sum float intra = val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, intra, offset); if (lane_id + offset < 32) intra += other; } // Total for this warp float warp_total = __reduce_add_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val); if (lane_id == 0) { shared[warp_id] = warp_total; } __syncthreads(); // Inclusive suffix sum of warp totals using warp 0 if (warp_id == 0) { float wval = (lane_id < 32) ? shared[lane_id] : 0.0f; float wsum = wval; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, wsum, offset); if (lane_id + offset < 32) wsum += other; } shared[lane_id] = wsum; } __syncthreads(); float extra = (warp_id < 31) ? shared[warp_id + 1] : 0.0f; float out_val = intra + extra + carry; row_out[col] = out_val; carry += shared[0]; // shared[0] now holds total sum of this chunk __syncthreads(); } } ``` This looks correct and elegant. Let's verify with a small example. Row: [a,b,c,d,...] processed in chunks of 4 for simplicity (blockSize=4, 1 warp). cols=8, blockSize=4. chunk_start = 4: cols 4,5,6,7. tid 0: val=x4, intra=x4+x5+x6+x7. tid 1: val=x5, intra=x5+x6+x7. etc. warp_total = x4+x5+x6+x7. stored in shared[0]. warp 0 computes wsum: shared[0] = x4+x5+x6+x7. extra = (warp_id < 0? no, warp_id=0, 0<0 false) => 0. (since only 1 warp) out_val = intra + 0 + carry(0). tid0 out: x4+x5+x6+x7. Correct (suffix at 4). tid1 out: x5+x6+x7. Correct. carry += shared[0] => carry = x4+x5+x6+x7. chunk_start = 0: cols 0,1,2,3. tid0: val=x0, intra=x0+x1+x2+x3. out_val = intra + 0 + carry = x0+x1+x2+x3 + (x4+...+x7). Correct, suffix at 0. tid1: x1+x2+x3 + carry. Correct. What about multiple warps? Let's say blockSize=8, 2 warps. cols=16. chunk_start=8: cols 8..15. warp 0: cols 8..11. intra_warp suffix computed. warp_total0 = sum(8..11). stored shared[0]. warp 1: cols 12..15. intra_warp suffix computed. warp_total1 = sum(12..15). stored shared[1]. Warp 0 computes inclusive suffix of [wt0, wt1]: lane0: wsum = wt0+wt1 = total chunk = shared[0]. lane1: wsum = wt1 = shared[1]. shared[0]=total, shared[1]=wt1. Now for warp 0 (warp_id=0): extra = shared[1] = wt1 = sum(12..15). tid0 (col8): out = intra(8..11) + extra(12..15) + carry. Correct. tid1 (col9): out = intra(9..11) + extra + carry. Correct. For warp 1 (warp_id=1): extra = (1<1)? no => 0. tid (col12): out = intra(12..15) + 0 + carry. Correct. carry += shared[0] = total chunk. Correct. Great! The logic holds. Let's refine the kernel: ```cuda #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void reverse_cumsum_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int rows, int cols) { __shared__ float shared[33]; // 32 warps int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= rows) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane_id = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; const float* row_in = input + (int64_t)row * cols; float* row_out = output + (int64_t)row * cols; float carry = 0.0f; for (int chunk_start = cols - blockDim.x; chunk_start >= 0; chunk_start -= blockDim.x) { int col = chunk_start + tid; float val = row_in[col]; // Step 1: inclusive suffix sum within warp float intra = val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, intra, offset); if (lane_id + offset < 32) { intra += other; } } // Step 2: warp total float warp_total = val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float other = __shfl_up_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, warp_total, offset); if (lane_id >= offset) warp_total += other; } // Wait, shfl_up does prefix sum. For total, easier: #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { warp_total += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, warp_total, offset); } // This gives total sum in all lanes. if (lane_id == 0) { shared[warp_id] = warp_total; } __syncthreads(); // Step 3: inclusive suffix sum of warp totals if (warp_id == 0) { float wval = (lane_id < 32) ? shared[lane_id] : 0.0f; float wsum = wval; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, wsum, offset); if (lane_id + offset < 32) wsum += other; } shared[lane_id] = wsum; } __syncthreads(); float extra = (warp_id < 31) ? shared[warp_id + 1] : 0.0f; row_out[col] = intra + extra + carry; carry += shared[0]; __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, I used `__shfl_down_sync` for warp_total reduction. Complete CUDA source: ```cuda #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <torch/extension.h> __global__ void reverse_cumsum_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int rows, int cols) { __shared__ float shared[33]; int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= rows) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane_id = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; const float* row_in = input + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols; float* row_out = output + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols; float carry = 0.0f; int num_chunks = (cols + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int chunk = num_chunks - 1; chunk >= 0; --chunk) { int chunk_start = chunk * blockDim.x; int col = chunk_start + tid; bool valid = col < cols; float val = valid ? row_in[col] : 0.0f; // inclusive suffix within warp float intra = val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, intra, offset); if (lane_id + offset < 32) intra += other; } // warp total reduction float warp_total = val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { warp_total += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, warp_total, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { shared[warp_id] = warp_total; } __syncthreads(); // inclusive suffix of warp totals in warp 0 if (warp_id == 0) { float wval = (lane_id < ((blockDim.x + 31) / 32)) ? Let's simplify the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void reverse_cumsum_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int rows, int cols) { __shared__ float shared[32]; int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= rows) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane_id = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; const float* row_in = input + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols; float* row_out = output + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols; float carry = 0.0f; int num_chunks = (cols + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x; for (int chunk = num_chunks - 1; chunk >= 0; --chunk) { int chunk_start = chunk * blockDim.x; int col = chunk_start + tid; bool valid = col < cols; float val = valid ? row_in[col] : 0.0f; float intra = val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, intra, offset); if (lane_id + offset < 32) intra += other; } float warp_total = val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { warp_total += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, warp_total, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { shared[warp_id] = warp_total; } __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float wval = shared[lane_id]; float wsum = wval; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset <<= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, wsum, offset); if (lane_id + offset < 32) wsum += other; } shared[lane_id] = wsum; } __syncthreads(); float extra = (warp_id < 31) ? shared[warp_id + 1] : 0.0f; if (valid) { row_out[col] = intra + extra + carry; } carry += shared[0]; __syncthreads(); } } ``` This is cleaner. Is there any issue with `shared` being accessed out of bounds when `warp_id >= 32`? Block size is 1024, so `warp_id` is 0..31. `lane_id` is 0..31. In `if (warp_id == 0)`, `lane_id` accesses `shared[lane_id]`, 0..31. All in bounds. In `if (lane_id == 0)`, `shared[warp_id]` for warp_id=0..31. In bounds. `warp_id < 31` accesses `shared[warp_id+1]` up to `shared[31]`. In bounds. Perfect. One more optimization: for very small `num_chunks` (e.g., 1), the loop overhead is small. Let row elements be a[0..N-1]. We want output[i] = sum_{j=i}^{N-1} a[j]. This is exactly prefix sum from right to left. Strategy: process each row with a block of threads. Each thread handles elements in a strided pattern. Use warp shuffle for intra-warp prefix sum. A common efficient prefix sum algorithm for 1D arrays per block: 1. Each thread loads multiple elements (e.g., 4 or 8 per thread) into registers, does a local prefix sum within its segment. 2. Write segment sums into shared memory. 3. Do a prefix sum on the segment sums (using warp shuffles or shared-memory scan). 4. Each thread adds the preceding segment sum to its local prefix values. 5. Write back to global memory. This is a standard "chunked" prefix sum. For a row of size 32768, we can have threads per block = 256, 512, or 1024. For maximal occupancy, maybe 512 or 256 is fine. More concretely, tile the row from right to left. Then the suffix sum for each element a[i] in this tile is: local_prefix_of_reversed_tile[i] + sum_of_all_elements_to_the_right_of_the_tile. The sum of all elements to the right of the tile is the total suffix sum of a[start+1] (the element immediately to the right of the tile's left boundary). Let's call this `carry`. Memory coalescing: within a warp, thread t loads addresses `cols - 1 - t*K`, etc. The addresses across threads t are spaced by K. CUB provides loading utilities like `LoadDirectBlocked` and `LoadDirectStriped`. For coalescing, `LoadDirectStriped` is better: each thread loads every `BLOCK_THREADS`-th element. In CUB, `BlockScan` uses the `T` items per thread in a "blocked" order: the first `ITEMS_PER_THREAD` items belong to thread 0, the next to thread 1, etc. This is the default arrangement. There are also "striped" variants if we specify an iterator with `CacheModifiedInputIterator` or use `LoadDirectStriped` / `StoreDirectStriped`. Wait, but we want perfect coalescing. There is a way: we can load in a striped fashion into shared memory, then transpose to registers in blocked order, then do BlockScan in blocked order, then transpose back to shared memory, and store striped. Alternatively, we can load in blocked order but use vectorized loads (e.g., float4) to improve coalescing. With `BLOCK_THREADS=512` and `ITEMS_PER_THREAD=64`, the blocked order means thread 0 reads addresses 0..63, thread 1 reads 64..127, etc. In a warp of 32 threads, thread 0 reads 0..3, thread 1 reads 64..67, etc. The addresses are 64 floats apart = 256 bytes. Within a warp, each thread reads a contiguous block of 256 bytes, but the blocks are widely separated. This is actually not terrible on modern GPUs with L1 cache, but it may cause some uncoalescing. Using striped load is much better for coalescing: thread `t` reads `t + k*BLOCK_THREADS`. For k=0, threads 0..31 read 0..31 (one cache line). For k=1, threads read 512..543, which is also coalesced. So striped load is the way to go for coalescing. To handle striped data with BlockScan, we can use `cub::BlockExchange` to transpose between striped and blocked layout in shared memory. This is exactly what CUB's `BlockLoad` and `BlockStore` with `BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE` and `BLOCK_STORE_WARP_TRANSPOSE` do. They load in striped order (coalesced), store to shared memory, transpose to blocked order in registers, then do the scan, then store back to shared memory, transpose to striped, and store to global memory. This is a standard CUB pattern and is highly efficient. So: 1. Use `cub::BlockLoad<float, BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD, BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE>` to load data coalesced from global memory. 2. This is coalesced. - We perform a local prefix sum within each thread's 128 elements (in the order they appear in the reversed sequence?). - Then we reduce the per-thread sums to get the prefix sum across threads. - Finally, each thread adds the offset. But the ordering is important. Let's define the reversed sequence index `r` from 0 to `cols-1`. Memory address = `cols - 1 - r`. If thread `t` handles reversed indices `r = t + k*BLOCK_THREADS` for `k=0..ITEMS_PER_THREAD-1`, then: - element 0 for thread t: `r = t`, memory = `cols-1-t`. - element 1 for thread t: `r = t+256`, memory = `cols-1-t-256`. So thread t loads: `cols-1-t`, `cols-1-t-256`, `cols-1-t-512`, ... These are descending by 256. Within a warp, threads 0..31 load: `cols-1`, `cols-2`, ..., `cols-32` for k=0. Perfect coalescing. For k=1, they load `cols-1-256`, ..., `cols-32-256`. Still perfect coalescing. So the logical reversed sequence is naturally striped: element r is at memory `cols-1-r`. If we map reversed index r to thread t and local item k via `r = t + k*BLOCK_THREADS`, then memory load is coalesced. Now we need to compute inclusive prefix sum on the reversed sequence. In the striped layout, thread t has items `t, t+BLOCK, t+2*BLOCK, ...`. The inclusive prefix sum of the whole sequence at position `r = t + k*BLOCK` is `sum_{i=0}^{r} reversed[i]`. We can compute this in two steps: 1. Compute local prefix sum within each thread's items. - For thread t: local items are `v_k = reversed[t + k*BLOCK]`. - Local prefix sum: `local_prefix[k] = sum_{j=0}^{k} v_j`. 2. Compute the prefix sum of the last element of each thread (i.e., the sum of all elements before the thread's segment). - Let `thread_sum[t] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} v_k = sum of reversed indices from t to t+(K-1)*BLOCK`. - Actually, the items assigned to thread t are not a contiguous block in the reversed sequence! They are strided by BLOCK. So indices are t, t+BLOCK, t+2*BLOCK, ... - The sum of all elements with reversed index < t is `sum_{i=0}^{t-1} reversed[i]`. These are t elements: indices 0,1,...,t-1, which are distributed among the first few threads with wrap-around in k. - This makes the cross-thread prefix sum tricky because each thread holds a strided subset, not a contiguous chunk. Ah, this is why CUB's BlockScan works with blocked layout: thread 0 holds items 0..K-1, thread 1 holds items K..2K-1, etc. In this layout, the cross-thread prefix sum is easy: the sum of all items in thread 0 is the offset for thread 1. In the striped layout, you need a transpose first. So to use the simple cross-thread prefix sum with warp shuffles, we should work in **blocked** layout and accept the potentially suboptimal coalescing, or use shared memory transposition. Given that the row size is 32768 and block size is 256, each thread handles 128 items. In blocked layout, thread t handles reversed indices `r = t*128` to `(t+1)*128 - 1`. The memory addresses are `cols-1 - (t*128 + k)`, meaning thread t loads `cols-1-t*128`, `cols-2-t*128`, ..., `cols-128-t*128`. So thread 0 loads the rightmost 128 elements, thread 1 loads the next 128, etc. Within a warp (32 threads), thread 0 loads addresses `cols-1` down to `cols-128`, thread 1 loads `cols-129` down to `cols-256`, etc. These are contiguous chunks interleaved by 128 elements. In terms of memory coalescing: - A warp consists of threads 0..31. - Their first loads are `cols-1`, `cols-129`, `cols-257`, ... (strided by 128 floats = 512 bytes). This is not contiguous within a warp. Each thread loads a contiguous 128 elements, but across threads there's a stride of 512 bytes. - The warp issues memory transactions of 32 bytes. So 32 threads * 4 bytes = 128 bytes per item if all read simultaneously, but they are scattered. For the first item (k=0), thread t reads address `cols-1-128*t`. The difference between thread 0 and thread 1 is 512 bytes. That's 16 cache lines apart for the first item. So the warp will need many transactions. This is very bad. So we definitely need coalesced loading. The way to do this is: 1. Load from global memory in coalesced order (striped) into shared memory. 2. Transpose in shared memory to blocked order in registers. 3. Do BlockScan. 4. Transpose back from blocked registers to shared memory in striped order. 5. Store to global memory. CUB provides exactly this via `BlockLoad` with `BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE` and `BlockStore` with `BLOCK_STORE_WARP_TRANSPOSE`. Let me write the kernel using CUB: ```cpp #include <cub/cub.cuh> template <int BLOCK_THREADS, int ITEMS_PER_THREAD> __global__ void reverse_cumsum_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int rows, int cols) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= rows) return; const float* row_in = input + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols; float* row_out = output + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols; using BlockLoad = cub::BlockLoad<float, BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD, cub::BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE>; using BlockStore = cub::BlockStore<float, BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD, cub::BLOCK_STORE_WARP_TRANSPOSE>; using BlockScan = cub::BlockScan<float, BLOCK_THREADS>; using TempStorage = union { typename BlockLoad::TempStorage load; typename BlockStore::TempStorage store; typename BlockScan::TempStorage scan; }; __shared__ TempStorage temp_storage; float thread_data[ITEMS_PER_THREAD]; int total_items = cols; int tile_size = BLOCK_THREADS * ITEMS_PER_THREAD; int num_tiles = (total_items + tile_size - 1) / tile_size; float carry = 0.0f; // Process tiles from right to left for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; ++tile) { int tile_offset = tile * tile_size; int remaining = total_items - tile_offset; int valid_items = min(remaining, tile_size); // Point to the rightmost element of this tile const float* tile_ptr = row_in + total_items - 1 - tile_offset; // Load this tile in coalesced order (striped from the end) // Since we're reading backwards, we need an iterator that goes backwards. // Alternatively, use a custom iterator. But CUB's BlockLoad takes a random-access // iterator. We can create a reverse iterator. // Actually, let's load manually in coalesced order. // Thread t loads tile_ptr - t, tile_ptr - t - BLOCK_THREADS, etc. // Then we need to layout this in blocked order for BlockScan. // BlockLoad with WARP_TRANSPOSE does exactly coalesced load + transpose to blocked. // But it expects a forward iterator. // We can pass tile_ptr - valid_items + 1 as the start and load forward, // but then the logical items are the leftmost part of the remaining array, // not the rightmost. // Since we process right-to-left, we are taking the rightmost tile first. // But `tile_ptr` points to the rightmost element. A forward iterator from // `tile_ptr - valid_items + 1` to `tile_ptr` would load the contiguous block // that makes up this tile, in forward order (left to right within the tile). // But we want to scan this tile in reverse order relative to the original row. // Wait: if we process from right to left, the first tile we handle is the // rightmost chunk of the original row. The reversed sequence of this chunk // is just the chunk read from right to left. // If we read it forward in memory (left to right within the tile), that's // the reverse of the reversed sequence. Hmm. // Let's think about the reversed sequence as a whole: b[r] = a[cols-1-r]. // The rightmost tile in original corresponds to r from tile_offset to tile_offset+valid_items-1. // b[r] for these r is a[cols-1-r] which ranges from a[cols-1-tile_offset] down to a[cols-1-tile_offset-valid_items+1]. // This is a contiguous block in memory! Let's verify: // r = tile_offset -> a[cols-1-tile_offset] // r = tile_offset+1 -> a[cols-2-tile_offset] // ... // r = tile_offset+valid_items-1 -> a[cols-valid_items-tile_offset] // These are contiguous addresses: from `row_in + cols - tile_offset - valid_items` to `row_in + cols - tile_offset - 1`. // And the reversed order (increasing r) corresponds to decreasing memory addresses. // But if we just treat the sequence b as an abstract array, the segment of b for this tile is `b[tile_offset .. tile_offset+valid_items-1]`. // Does this segment correspond to a contiguous block in memory? Yes! Because b[r] = row_in + cols - 1 - r. // b[tile_offset + k] = row_in + cols - 1 - tile_offset - k. // So the start of this block in memory is `row_in + cols - tile_offset - valid_items`, and end is `row_in + cols - tile_offset - 1`. // If we define `tile_start_ptr = row_in + cols - tile_offset - valid_items`, // then `b[tile_offset + k] = tile_start_ptr[valid_items - 1 - k]`. // This means the segment of b IS contiguous in memory but in reverse order. // If we use BlockLoad to load `valid_items` elements from `tile_start_ptr` forward, // we get `tile_start_ptr[0], tile_start_ptr[1], ...` which correspond to // `b[tile_offset + valid_items - 1], b[tile_offset + valid_items - 2], ...`. // This is exactly the reversed tile sequence! So the logical items we load // are `b[tile_offset + valid_items - 1 - i]`. // We want to scan these items in the order they logically appear in b, // which is `b[tile_offset], b[tile_offset+1], ...`. // The loaded order is the reverse of that! // So we would need to reverse the local array within each thread after loading. // That seems messy. } } ``` This is getting complicated. Let's think differently. The CUB `DeviceScan` can scan an entire array, but that doesn't help with 2D rows easily. Another idea: since we have many rows, we can use `torch.cumsum` but maybe implement a custom kernel that's simply faster by fusing the `flip` + `cumsum` + `flip` and avoiding temporary allocations. The PyTorch reference does: 1. `flip(dim)` - creates a temp tensor. 2. `cumsum(dim)` - creates another temp tensor. 3. `flip(dim)` - creates final output. A fused kernel avoids all temporaries and all the overhead of launching 3 separate kernels. Even if the kernel's raw compute is similar, saving memory bandwidth from not reading/writing temporaries can give a speedup. This is likely the easiest path to victory. We can write a very simple kernel: One thread per element. For element (i, j), output[i, j] = sum_{k=j}^{cols-1} input[i, k]. We can compute this with each thread doing a serial summation? No, 32768 elements per row with 32768 threads per row is too many? Actually, 32768x32768 = 1B elements. We can't have each thread independently sum up to 32768 elements; that's O(N^2) work. We need parallel scan per row. Simpler manual approach: A two-pass algorithm per row. - Pass 1: compute segment sums (e.g., each warp sums a contiguous chunk). - Pass 2: compute prefix sums of segment sums to get offsets. - Pass 3: each warp/thread computes local prefix sums and adds offset. Let's do a manual blocked scan with coalesced loads via shared memory. I'll design it carefully. Details: - Block size: 256 threads. - Items per thread: 16 (or 32). This is called the "strip-mining" factor. - Total items per block: 256 * 16 = 4096. - Number of tiles per row: ceil(32768 / 4096) = 8. Each block processes one row in 8 tiles from right to left. For a single tile (4096 elements from original row): - These elements are contiguous in the original row (since items per row are contiguous). - In the reversed sequence, this tile corresponds to a contiguous block but traversed backwards. - Actually, as noted before, a contiguous block of the original row `[base, base+4095]` corresponds to reversed indices `[cols-1-base-4095, ..., cols-1-base]`. - Wait, if the tile in original is `[start, start+4095]`, then in reversed sequence: `r = cols - 1 - idx`. As idx goes from `start` to `start+4095`, r goes from `cols-1-start` down to `cols-1-start-4095`. So this is a contiguous block in r-space: `[cols-1-start-4095, cols-1-start]`. It is contiguous and in increasing r order is decreasing memory order. Since we process tiles from right to left in original space, the first tile is the rightmost: `[cols-4096, cols-1]`. This corresponds to reversed r block `[0, 4095]`. The next tile is `[cols-8192, cols-4097]`, corresponding to r block `[4096, 8191]`. So tile 0 in r-space is `[0, 4095]`, tile 1 is `[4096, 8191]`, etc. THIS IS KEY! If we process the row in chunks from right to left in original memory, these chunks map to contiguous chunks in the reversed sequence space, in left-to-right order. - Tile 0 (rightmost original chunk) = reversed indices 0..4095. - Tile 1 (next original chunk to the left) = reversed indices 4096..8191. - etc. This is perfect! Because then: - For tile `t`, the offset in r-space is `t * tile_size`. - The elements of tile `t` in r-space are contiguous: `offset` to `offset + tile_size - 1`. - In memory, these elements are at addresses `row_in + cols - 1 - offset` down to `row_in + cols - offset - tile_size`. This is a contiguous block of size `tile_size` in memory but in reverse order. - However, if we load this memory block in forward order (from `row_in + cols - offset - tile_size` to `row_in + cols - offset - 1`), we get the reversed sequence of this tile! That is, the first element loaded is `r = offset + tile_size - 1`, which is the LAST element of the tile in r-space. So we can either: - Load the memory block forward, reverse the local array to get r-order, then scan. - OR, load the memory block backward (using a reverse iterator), getting r-order directly. But loading backward breaks coalescing! Because thread 0 would read the last address, thread 1 the second-to-last, etc. That's actually coalesced too! Let's check: - Memory addresses for threads 0..31: `last, last-1, last-2, ...`. These are contiguous descending. GPUs handle descending contiguous just as well as ascending contiguous. Yes! Contiguous memory access in either direction is coalesced. So we can simply have each thread load from descending addresses. So: - Base pointer for tile `t`: `base = row_in + cols - 1 - t * tile_size`. - Thread `tid` loads `base - tid*ITEMS_PER_THREAD - (ITEMS_PER_THREAD-1)` up to `base - tid*ITEMS_PER_THREAD`. Or more cleanly: thread `tid` handles elements `base - (tid+1)*ITEMS_PER_THREAD + 1` through `base - tid*ITEMS_PER_THREAD`. Wait, to have thread 0 handle the first items in r-space (i.e., the rightmost elements), and contiguous within a warp... r-space items: `r = offset` to `offset + tile_size - 1`. Memory: `m = row_in + cols - 1 - r`. As `r` increases, `m` decreases. Thread `tid` should handle r's = `offset + tid*ITEMS_PER_THREAD` to `offset + (tid+1)*ITEMS_PER_THREAD - 1`. The memory addresses for these are `m_start = row_in + cols - 1 - (offset + (tid+1)*ITEMS_PER_THREAD - 1)` = `row_in + cols - offset - (tid+1)*ITEMS_PER_THREAD`. And `m_end = row_in + cols - 1 - (offset + tid*ITEMS_PER_THREAD)` = `row_in + cols - offset - tid*ITEMS_PER_THREAD - 1`. So thread `tid` accesses memory block `[m_start, m_end]` (inclusive), which is of length `ITEMS_PER_THREAD`. These blocks for consecutive threads are consecutive in memory! Let's verify: - Thread 0: `[row_in + cols - offset - ITEMS, row_in + cols - offset - 1]`. - Thread 1: `[row_in + cols - offset - 2*ITEMS, row_in + cols - offset - ITEMS - 1]`. Yes, they are adjacent blocks in memory, with thread 1's block immediately to the left of thread 0's block. So within a warp, thread 0 accesses addresses `end-0`, thread 1 accesses `end-ITEMS`, etc. Wait, no: thread 0 accesses the HIGHEST addresses (closest to the end of the row), thread 1 accesses the next block down. In terms of coalescing: within a warp, the 32 threads access 32 blocks of `ITEMS_PER_THREAD` floats each. If `ITEMS_PER_THREAD` is 1, thread 0 reads `end`, thread 1 reads `end-1`, etc., perfectly contiguous. Great! If `ITEMS_PER_THREAD` > 1, then each thread reads a small contiguous chunk, and these chunks are adjacent. This is also very coalesced (almost as good as `float4` loads). So this layout is actually "blocked" in memory but corresponds to blocked layout in r-space if we read from high address to low address within the thread. Wait, does CUB's BlockScan work with this? In the blocked layout, thread `t` owns items `t*ITEMS` to `(t+1)*ITEMS - 1`. We want thread `t` to own r-items `t*ITEMS` to `(t+1)*ITEMS - 1`. Since r and memory are reversed, thread `t`'s memory block is at `cols-1-((t+1)*ITEMS-1)` down to `cols-1-(t*ITEMS)`. This is exactly a blocked memory layout! So we don't need any transpose at all! We can simply use CUB's BlockScan directly, with the data already in the correct blocked layout. Let's verify the coalescing of this blocked memory load: - Warp 0 has threads 0..31. - Thread t loads memory addresses: `base_t = row_in + cols - 1 - ((t+1)*ITEMS - 1)` for the first item. For `ITEMS = 4`: thread 0 loads `row_in + cols - 4`, thread 1 loads `row_in + cols - 8`, etc. So the warp loads addresses: `cols-4`, `cols-8`, `cols-12`, ... which are strided by 16 bytes (`4*4`). On modern GPUs, a warp loads 32 threads * 4 bytes = 128 bytes. With stride 16 bytes, the addresses are `cols-4, cols-8, cols-12, ... cols-128`. This is still within a contiguous 128-byte range (if we reorder them: `cols-128` to `cols-4`). Actually, the addresses span from `cols-128` to `cols-4`, which is 124 bytes. So a single 128-byte transaction can cover all of them. So it's fully coalesced into one transaction! - Let's check `ITEMS = 1`: addresses are `cols-1, cols-2, ..., cols-32`. Contiguous descending. One transaction. - `ITEMS = 16`: addresses for first element of each thread: `cols-16, cols-32, ..., cols-512`. Span = 496 bytes. That's 4 transactions of 128 bytes. Still very good. And within each thread, the 16 loads are contiguous in memory (descending by 1). So overall coalescing is excellent. Wait, there's a subtlety: if each thread does a loop over its `ITEMS_PER_THREAD` elements loading one by one, then for a given iteration `k` (0-indexed), thread `t` loads address `base_t - k` where `base_t = row_in + cols - 1 - (t+1)*ITEMS + 1` = `row_in + cols - t*ITEMS - 1`? Let's recalculate. Thread `t` owns r-items: `r_start = t*ITEMS` to `r_end = (t+1)*ITEMS - 1`. Memory addresses: `m_start = row_in + cols - 1 - r_end` = `row_in + cols - (t+1)*ITEMS`. `m_end = row_in + cols - 1 - r_start` = `row_in + cols - 1 - t*ITEMS`. So thread `t` reads addresses from `m_start` to `m_end` (inclusive), which is `ITEMS` addresses. These are increasing memory addresses? Wait: `m_start = row_in + cols - (t+1)*ITEMS`. `m_end = row_in + cols - 1 - t*ITEMS`. Since `m_end > m_start`, yes, it's a block of `ITEMS` contiguous floats. But which direction? The r-order is from `r_start` to `r_end`, which maps memory from `m_end` down to `m_start`. So in r-order, thread `t` reads `m_end, m_end-1, ..., m_start`. If we store these as `thread_data[0] = reversed[r_start]`, `thread_data[1] = reversed[r_start+1]`, etc., then: `thread_data[0] = *(m_end)`, `thread_data[1] = *(m_end - 1)`, ... `thread_data[ITEMS-1] = *(m_start)`. So within a thread, memory is traversed backward. For coalescing, across a warp (32 threads), at load `k` of the loop: - Thread `t` reads `m_end - k = row_in + cols - 1 - t*ITEMS - k`. - For `k=0`: `row_in + cols - 1 - t*ITEMS`. These are spaced by `ITEMS` floats. - For `ITEMS=16`, the spacing is 64 bytes. 32 threads read addresses: `cols-1`, `cols-17`, `cols-33`, ..., `cols-497`. The span is 496 bytes. As said, 4 transactions of 128 bytes. Good. - For `ITEMS=1`, spacing is 4 bytes, span is 124 bytes, 1 transaction. This is perfectly acceptable coalescing. Now, for CUB BlockScan blocked layout: it expects `thread_data[k]` to correspond to the `(t*ITEMS + k)`-th item in the sequence. So we can load it exactly as described above. For multiple tiles: we process tiles from right to left in original memory. Tile 0 covers reversed r-indices `[0, tile_size-1]`. Tile 1 covers `[tile_size, 2*tile_size-1]`, etc. Wait, does the rightmost original chunk map to the smallest r-indices? Yes! Because `r = cols - 1 - idx`. As `idx` goes from `cols-tile_size` to `cols-1` (rightmost chunk), `r` goes from `cols-1-(cols-1)=0` down to `cols-1-(cols-tile_size)=tile_size-1`. So rightmost original chunk = smallest r-indices. This is perfect because: - Tile 0 (rightmost chunk) = r `[0, tile_size-1]`. We scan this tile. The carry-in for this tile is 0. - Tile 1 (next chunk left) = r `[tile_size, 2*tile_size-1]`. We scan this tile. The carry-in is the total sum of tile 0 (which is the block_aggregate from scanning tile 0). - And so on. Wait, we need the suffix sum (inclusive scan on reversed). For tile `t` covering r-indices `[t*tile_size, (t+1)*tile_size-1]`, the inclusive scan values within this tile need to include the sum of all reversed elements with smaller r-index, i.e., the sum of all tiles `< t`. So yes, tile `t`'s scan needs carry = sum of tiles `0..t-1`. But we're processing tiles from right to left in original memory, which corresponds to increasing `t` in r-space. So tile 0 first, then tile 1, etc. The carry accumulates. But wait, `BlockScan::InclusiveSum` computes the inclusive scan within the tile and also returns the aggregate sum of the tile. However, if `valid_items < tile_size`, we need to handle partial tiles carefully. CUB has `BlockScan::InclusiveSum` with a partial tile parameter using `valid_items`. Yes, there is an overload: `BlockScan(temp_storage).InclusiveSum(thread_data, thread_data, valid_items, block_aggregate)`. Wait, CUB's BlockScan docs show an overload with a `int num_valid` for partial tiles? Actually, standard BlockScan works on the full array of `BLOCK_THREADS * ITEMS_PER_THREAD`. For partial tiles, we can set out-of-bounds elements to 0 and then do a full scan, but the aggregate would include the padded zeros. Alternatively, we use `BlockScan::InclusiveSum` with a `TilePrefixCallbackOp` for prefix sums, but for just a block-wide scan, I don't think there's a direct `num_valid` overload. Actually, CUB has: ```cpp __device__ __forceinline__ void InclusiveSum( T (&input)[ITEMS_PER_THREAD], T (&output)[ITEMS_PER_THREAD]); ``` and another with `block_aggregate` output. But it doesn't directly take a `valid_items` count. However, for our toy case, `cols = 32768`. If we choose `BLOCK_THREADS = 256` and `ITEMS_PER_THREAD = 128`, then `tile_size = 32768`, so exactly one tile per row! This simplifies everything massively. And it's very reasonable: 256 threads * 128 items = 32768. Each thread holds 128 floats in registers (512 bytes). Total registers per block = 256 * 128 * 4 bytes = 131072 bytes? Wait no, registers are per-thread. Each thread uses 128 floats = 128 registers. That's a lot. NVCC might spill. Let's calculate: 128 float registers per thread. A float register is 4 bytes, but each register is 4 bytes. 128 registers per thread is the typical limit. Actually, modern GPUs have 255 registers per thread. 128 is fine, but 128 * 4 = 512 bytes, okay. Wait, 128 * 4 bytes = 512 bytes. But a register is 4 bytes. 128 registers is just 128 registers. Many compute kernels use 128 or more registers. It should be okay. Let me check: 256 threads, 128 registers each -> 32768 registers per block. Each SM has 65536 registers. So two blocks can fit per SM. Occupancy = 256 * 2 = 512 threads out of 2048 possible. That's 25% occupancy due to registers. But the kernel is very memory-light (128KB of reads + 128KB of writes per block). Once per row. Actually, 128 registers might cause NVCC to use more due to temp variables. Let's try `ITEMS_PER_THREAD = 64`. Then `tile_size = 256 * 64 = 16384`. We need 2 tiles per row (32768 / 16384 = 2). That's still very manageable. Each thread uses 64 floats -> 64 registers. 256 * 64 = 16384 registers per block. Then 4 blocks per SM. Occupancy = 1024 threads = 50%. That's better. With 2 tiles per row, carry propagation is trivial: just save the aggregate of tile 0 and pass as prefix to tile 1. Let's go with `BLOCK_THREADS = 256`, `ITEMS_PER_THREAD = 64`, `tile_size = 16384`. Second tile is partial? No, 2*16384 = 32768, exactly. Wait, is there enough shared memory for CUB BlockScan? CUB BlockScan shared memory is typically `BLOCK_THREADS` sized or a few KB. For 256 threads, it's around 1-2KB. Plus load/store temp storage might be another few KB. Total < 8KB. So fine. Let's implement this. Template parameters: - `BLOCK_THREADS = 256` - `ITEMS_PER_THREAD = 64` Kernel flow: 1. row = blockIdx.x. 2. row_in = input + row * cols. 3. output = output + row * cols. 4. tile_size = BLOCK_THREADS * ITEMS_PER_THREAD = 16384. 5. num_tiles = (cols + tile_size - 1) / tile_size = 2. 6. carry = 0.0f. 7. For tile_idx = 0..num_tiles-1: a. offset_r = tile_idx * tile_size. b. valid_items = min(tile_size, cols - offset_r). c. Memory start for this tile: `m_block_start = row_in + cols - (offset_r + valid_items)`. Wait: we established that reversed r-item `r` is at memory `row_in + cols - 1 - r`. The block of reversed r-items `[offset_r, offset_r + valid_items)` maps to memory block: start: `row_in + cols - 1 - (offset_r + valid_items - 1)` = `row_in + cols - offset_r - valid_items`. end: `row_in + cols - 1 - offset_r`. These are exactly `valid_items` contiguous floats. d. Thread `t` owns r-items: `r = offset_r + t*ITEMS_PER_THREAD` to `offset_r + (t+1)*ITEMS_PER_THREAD - 1`. But if this exceeds `offset_r + valid_items`, we pad with 0. Memory for thread t: `m_t_start = row_in + cols - offset_r - (t+1)*ITEMS_PER_THREAD`. `m_t_end = row_in + cols - offset_r - 1 - t*ITEMS_PER_THREAD`. Actually let's be careful: r_start_t = offset_r + t*ITEMS_PER_THREAD. r_end_t = min(offset_r + (t+1)*ITEMS_PER_THREAD, offset_r + valid_items) - 1. m_start_t = row_in + cols - 1 - r_end_t. m_end_t = row_in + cols - 1 - r_start_t. The number of valid items for thread t is `r_end_t - r_start_t + 1`. If this is less than ITEMS_PER_THREAD, pad with 0. e. Load the valid items into `thread_data[k]` in r-order (0..ITEMS_PER_THREAD-1). For k from 0 to ITEMS_PER_THREAD-1: r = r_start_t + k. if r <= r_end_t: thread_data[k] = row_in[cols - 1 - r]; else: thread_data[k] = 0.0f; Note: `row_in[cols - 1 - r]` = `*(row_in + cols - 1 - r)`. Since `r` increases, memory address decreases. f. Perform BlockScan::InclusiveSum(thread_data, thread_data). g. Add `carry` to each `thread_data[k]`. h. Write back to output: For k from 0 to num_valid-1: r = r_start_t + k. output[cols - 1 - r] = thread_data[k]; (Since `output` is just another pointer, same indexing as input.) i. Compute block aggregate sum. BlockScan::InclusiveSum has an overload returning aggregate: `BlockScan(temp_storage).InclusiveSum(thread_data, thread_data, aggregate);` Wait, looking at CUB API: There is: ```cpp __device__ __forceinline__ void InclusiveSum( T (&input)[ITEMS_PER_THREAD], T (&output)[ITEMS_PER_THREAD], T &block_aggregate); ``` Yes, the third parameter gives the total sum of the tile. j. `carry += aggregate;`. Wait, step 8: Since we padded invalid items with 0, the aggregate correctly reflects the sum of valid items. One issue: we need to make sure `valid_items` is correctly handled. In our case, `cols = 32768`, `tile_size = 16384`, `num_tiles = 2`, and both tiles are exactly full. So padding is not needed. But the kernel should be general. Now, a key concern: the memory indexing formula `row_in[cols - 1 - r]`. Since `r = offset_r + t*ITEMS_PER_THREAD + k`, we have: `global_idx = row * cols + cols - 1 - offset_r - t*ITEMS_PER_THREAD - k`. For a fixed `row`, `offset_r`, and `k` (loop iteration), the address accessed by thread `t` is: `base - t*ITEMS_PER_THREAD` where `base = row*cols + cols - 1 - offset_r - k`. So for a warp of 32 threads, addresses are `base, base-ITEMS, base-2*ITEMS, ...`. With `ITEMS=64`, spacing is 256 bytes. For 32 threads, span is 32*256 = 8192 bytes = 64 cache lines (128 bytes each). On H100, L1 cache line is 128 bytes. A warp load instruction for 32 threads, each loading 4 bytes, with addresses strided by 256 bytes: the cache lines touched are `base/128`, `(base-256)/128`, etc. Since 256 is a multiple of 128, each thread touches a different cache line (if base is aligned). So 32 threads, 32 cache lines. That's a lot of transactions. However, the L1 cache might handle this, but it's not optimal. Can we do better? To optimize coalescing further, we should use `float4` vector loads. If each thread loads `float4` (4 floats = 16 bytes) as its `ITEMS_PER_THREAD` unit, the stride between threads is `64 * 4 = 256` floats? No. Wait, if we change ITEMS_PER_THREAD to mean number of vector elements. Let's say each thread processes 16 `float4`s. Then `ITEMS_PER_THREAD = 16` vectors = 64 floats. The stride between threads is `16 * 16 = 256` bytes. Still the same issue. Actually, the strided access pattern is inherent in a blocked layout where each thread holds a chunk. To get perfect coalescing, we need a striped layout, which is what CUB's `BlockLoad` with `BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE` achieves. Let me reconsider CUB BlockLoad transposed. If we use `BlockLoad` to load from memory in a coalesced way (striped) and transposes into blocked registers, then we get the best of both worlds. For our case: - We have a contiguous memory block of `valid_items` floats. - We want to load them into blocked register layout where thread `t` has items `t*ITEMS` to `(t+1)*ITEMS - 1` of the *reversed* sequence. - The reversed sequence for this block corresponds to memory traversed backwards. - CUB's `BlockLoad` takes an input iterator and loads in a coalesced manner. It expects the items in memory to be in the same forward order as the sequence. - If we pass a pointer to the start of the memory block and load forward, we get the sequence in the order of increasing memory address. - But our reversed sequence is in decreasing memory address. - We can define a custom iterator that dereferences to `ptr[-idx]`. - Does CUB support custom iterators? Yes, `BlockLoad` is a template on `InputIteratorT`. It can be any random-access iterator. - So we can define a simple `ReverseIterator` class. Let me write a minimal reverse iterator: ```cpp struct ReversePtr { const float* ptr; __device__ __forceinline__ float operator[](int i) const { return ptr[-i]; } __device__ __forceinline__ ReversePtr operator+(int i) const { return {ptr - i}; } }; ``` Wait, CUB iterators need to support `operator*`, `operator[]`, `operator+`, `operator++`, `operator-`, etc. A simple pointer-like struct: ```cpp class ReverseIterator { public: const float* ptr; __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator operator+(int offset) const { return ReverseIterator{ptr - offset}; } __device__ __forceinline__ float operator*() const { return *ptr; } __device__ __forceinline__ float operator[](int offset) const { return ptr[-offset]; } // wait, if ptr advances by offset, we want *(ptr - offset)? Let's think. }; ``` Actually, if `it = ReverseIterator{base}`, then `it[0] = *base`, `it[1] = *(base - 1)`, `it[k] = *(base - k)`. For `BlockLoad`, if we pass `it`, then it will access `it[0], it[1], ...` for coalesced loading (which corresponds to `base[0], base[-1], base[-2], ...`). The threads will load `base[0], base[1], base[2], ...` if BlockLoad uses blocked order. Wait, no. CUB BlockLoad with `BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE`: - It loads `valid_items` elements from the input iterator. - Thread `t` loads items that are coalesced in memory, then transposes to blocked layout. - If we want the blocked register item 0 of thread `t` to correspond to reversed sequence index `t*ITEMS`, which maps to memory `base_mem - t*ITEMS`, then after transpose, the memory access pattern is: warp loads elements `0, 1, 2, ...` from the iterator (ascending iterator index). If the iterator's `operator[]` returns `base_mem - idx`, then the actual memory accesses are `base_mem, base_mem-1, base_mem-2, ...`. But BlockLoad assumes contiguous increasing iterator index for coalescing, so for a warp, it loads `idx = warp_offset + laneid`, i.e., contiguous indices. The actual memory accessed would be `base_mem - (warp_offset + laneid)`, which is contiguous descending. That's perfectly coalesced! So yes, a reverse iterator where `it[i] = base_ptr[-i]` works. Then: - Use `BlockLoad` with this reverse iterator. - Use `BlockScan` on the blocked registers. - Use `BlockStore` with a reverse output iterator to write back in blocked order from registers to memory `base_ptr[-idx]`. Wait, does CUB have `BlockStore` that takes an output iterator? Yes. We can define a similar reverse iterator for the output pointer. So the sequence becomes: 1. Define `base_ptr = row_in + cols - 1 - offset_r`. (Points to the highest memory address of this tile). 2. `ReverseInputIterator in_it{base_ptr};` 3. `BlockLoad(temp_storage.load).Load(in_it, thread_data, valid_items, 0.0f);` This loads `valid_items` elements from `base_ptr[0], base_ptr[-1], ...` in coalesced order and transposes to blocked registers. 4. `BlockScan(temp_storage.scan).InclusiveSum(thread_data, thread_data, aggregate);` 5. Add `carry` to each `thread_data[k]` that is valid. Wait, `thread_data` contains padded 0s for invalid items. After inclusive sum, the valid items are correct, but the invalid items contain the sum of valid items in that thread (or part of it). We don't want to add carry to invalid items? Actually if we use `Load` with padding value 0, and then do inclusive sum, the padded items will get some values. We shouldn't write them back. When we store, we can use `BlockStore` with `valid_items` to only write valid ones. Or we can just not care because we overwrite with correct values in subsequent tiles? No, we shouldn't write garbage for invalid items. `BlockStore` supports `Store(out_it, thread_data, valid_items)`. It will only write the valid ones. 6. For the items that *are* valid, add `carry`. Actually, `BlockScan::InclusiveSum` includes the padded zeros in the scan, so valid items get the correct local prefix sum. Adding `carry` to all items (including invalid padded ones) is fine, but when we store only `valid_items`, the garbage won't be written. However, the padded items might have values equal to the aggregate of the valid items in that thread. Adding carry to them just shifts them, but they are still written only if we had more valid items. Since we store with `valid_items`, it's fine. Wait, but what if `valid_items` covers a partial thread? `BlockStore` with `valid_items` handles that correctly: only the first `valid_items` logical items are written. The rest are ignored. So we can simply add `carry` to all `thread_data[k]`. 7. `ReverseOutputIterator out_it{base_ptr};` `BlockStore(temp_storage.store).Store(out_it, thread_data, valid_items);` 8. `carry += aggregate;` This is very clean and efficient! And CUB should generate highly optimized code. One issue: the shared memory temp storage for BlockLoad, BlockScan, and BlockStore needs to be a union because they share the shared memory. Let's write the kernel using CUB: ```cpp #include <cub/cub.cuh> struct ReverseIterator { float* ptr; __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator operator+(int offset) const { return ReverseIterator{ptr - offset}; } __device__ __forceinline__ float& operator*() const { return *ptr; } __device__ __forceinline__ float& operator[](int offset) const { return ptr[-offset]; } }; struct ConstReverseIterator { const float* ptr; __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator operator+(int offset) const { return ConstReverseIterator{ptr - offset}; } __device__ __forceinline__ const float& operator*() const { return *ptr; } __device__ __forceinline__ const float& operator[](int offset) const { return ptr[-offset]; } }; ``` Wait, we need `operator[]` or `operator*`? Typically, it supports `InputIteratorT` which requires `operator*`, `operator++`, `operator[]` (random access). If `input` is our iterator, `input + offset` must return an iterator, and then `*(input + offset)` or `(input + offset)[i]`. Let's define all operators. Wait, for `BlockLoad`, the first parameter is `InputIteratorT block_itr`. CUB internally uses `LoadDirectBlocked`, `LoadDirectStriped`, etc. For `BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE`, I think it uses `LoadDirectStriped` which computes `block_itr + threadIdx.x` and then loads with stride `BLOCK_THREADS`. It might use `(block_itr + threadIdx.x)[k * BLOCK_THREADS]`. So we need: ```cpp __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator operator+(int offset) const; __device__ __forceinline__ float operator[](int offset) const; // Since block_itr + total_offset, then [sub_offset] ``` And actually if we define `operator[]` as `ptr[-offset]`, then: `(it + 5)[3] = (ptr - 5)[-3] = ptr[-5-3] = ptr[-8]`. But mathematically it should be: `it` points to logical index 0 at `ptr[0]`, `it+5` points to logical index 5 at `ptr[-5]`, then `(it+5)[3]` should be logical index 8 at `ptr[-8]`. Yes, `ptr[-(5+3)] = ptr[-8]`. So `operator[]` on the iterator itself should just be `ptr[-offset]`. Wait, but if we do `it + total_offset`, we get a new iterator with `ptr - total_offset`. Then `[sub_offset]` on that iterator returns `(ptr - total_offset)[-sub_offset] = ptr - total_offset - sub_offset`. This corresponds to logical index `total_offset + sub_offset`. Yes, correct. So: ```cpp struct ConstReverseIterator { const float* ptr; __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator operator+(int offset) const { return ConstReverseIterator{ptr - offset}; } __device__ __forceinline__ float operator[](int offset) const { return ptr[-offset]; } }; ``` And similar for mutable `ReverseIterator` with `float&`. Let's define mutable for output. Now, in the kernel, we need to handle the shared memory. CUB's `BlockLoad`, `BlockScan`, `BlockStore` each have a `TempStorage` type. We can create a union or use ` cub::detail::BlockLoadInternal` etc. Actually, the standard CUB samples show using a union: ```cpp using BlockLoad = cub::BlockLoad<float, BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD, cub::BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE>; using BlockStore = cub::BlockStore<float, BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD, cub::BLOCK_STORE_WARP_TRANSPOSE>; using BlockScan = cub::BlockScan<float, BLOCK_THREADS>; union TempStorage { typename BlockLoad::TempStorage load; typename BlockStore::TempStorage store; typename BlockScan::TempStorage scan; }; __shared__ TempStorage temp_storage; ``` Great. Let me design the code: In `.cu` source: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cub/cub.cuh> struct ConstReverseIterator { const float* ptr; __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator operator+(int offset) const { return ConstReverseIterator{ptr - offset}; } __device__ __forceinline__ float operator[](int offset) const { return ptr[-offset]; } }; struct ReverseIterator { float* ptr; __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator operator+(int offset) const { return ReverseIterator{ptr - offset}; } __device__ __forceinline__ float& operator[](int offset) const { return ptr[-offset]; } }; template <int BLOCK_THREADS, int ITEMS_PER_THREAD> __global__ void reverse_cumsum_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int rows, int cols) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= rows) return; const float* row_in = input + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols; float* row_out = output + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols; using BlockLoad = cub::BlockLoad<float, BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD, cub::BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE>; using BlockStore = cub::BlockStore<float, BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD, cub::BLOCK_STORE_WARP_TRANSPOSE>; using BlockScan = cub::BlockScan<float, BLOCK_THREADS>; union TempStorage { typename BlockLoad::TempStorage load; typename BlockStore::TempStorage store; typename BlockScan::TempStorage scan; }; __shared__ TempStorage temp_storage; float thread_data[ITEMS_PER_THREAD]; const int tile_size = BLOCK_THREADS * ITEMS_PER_THREAD; int num_tiles = (cols + tile_size - 1) / tile_size; float carry = 0.0f; for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; ++tile) { int offset_r = tile * tile_size; int valid_items = min(tile_size, cols - offset_r); ConstReverseIterator in_it{row_in + cols - 1 - offset_r}; BlockLoad(temp_storage.load).Load(in_it, thread_data, valid_items, 0.0f); __syncthreads(); float aggregate; BlockScan(temp_storage.scan).InclusiveSum(thread_data, thread_data, aggregate); // Add carry to valid items. Since padded items won't be stored, // we can add to all to keep it simple. #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ITEMS_PER_THREAD; ++i) { thread_data[i] += carry; } __syncthreads(); ReverseIterator out_it{row_out + cols - 1 - offset_r}; BlockStore(temp_storage.store).Store(out_it, thread_data, valid_items); carry += aggregate; __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, there is a subtle issue: `BlockLoad` and `BlockStore` with `BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE` and `BLOCK_STORE_WARP_TRANSPOSE` use shared memory for the transpose. The `TempStorage` types contain shared memory arrays. After loading, we call `__syncthreads()` before using the shared memory for scan, and after scan before using for store. CUB requires that we sync between operations using the same shared memory. Wait, actually, `BlockLoad` with warp transpose writes the loaded data into shared memory, then threads read from shared memory into registers. But `BlockLoad::Load` handles this internally and may use shared memory from its `TempStorage`. After `Load` returns, the data is in registers. But the shared memory used by `load` might be reused. The CUB docs say that if you alias the temp storage (via union), you must sync between uses. So `__syncthreads()` is needed. The `BlockScan::InclusiveSum` reads from registers, computes, writes back to registers. Wait, `BlockScan` also uses shared memory for warp-aggregates. So it needs its own `TempStorage` (which we provided via `temp_storage.scan`). And after it returns, the result is in registers. Then we add carry in registers. Then we call `BlockStore`, which writes registers to shared memory, then writes to global memory. So the sequence: 1. `BlockLoad(temp_storage.load).Load(...);` // uses shared memory internally 2. `__syncthreads();` // because next step uses shared memory via union 3. `BlockScan(temp_storage.scan).InclusiveSum(...);` // uses shared memory 4. Do register arithmetic. 5. `__syncthreads();` // because next step uses shared memory via union 6. `BlockStore(temp_storage.store).Store(...);` Yes. In `BlockScan::InclusiveSum`, does it read from registers? Yes. It takes `thread_data` array as input and output. It operates on registers. It uses shared memory only for temporary communication (prefix sums of warp aggregates, etc.). When we call `BlockScan(temp_storage.scan).InclusiveSum(thread_data, thread_data, aggregate);`, do we need a `__syncthreads()` after? Only before the next shared memory use. In our loop, the next iteration starts with `BlockLoad`, which uses the same shared memory union. So before the next `BlockLoad`, we need `__syncthreads()`. But before the `BlockStore`, we also need `__syncthreads()` because `BlockScan` used shared memory (via `temp_storage.scan`) and `BlockStore` will use `temp_storage.store` which is aliased in the same union. So yes, step 5 is `__syncthreads();`. But wait, `BlockStore` writes to global memory. Do we need `__syncthreads()` after it? Before the next loop iteration, we need all threads to have finished the store so that `temp_storage` can be reused. However, `BlockStore` might use shared memory, but once it finishes writing to global memory, the shared memory is no longer needed. The `carry` update is in registers. Then the loop goes back to `BlockLoad`. I think `__syncthreads()` at the top of the loop is sufficient, or at the end of the loop body. Actually, if `BlockStore` uses `temp_storage.store` and we immediately use `temp_storage.load` in the next iteration, the shared memory is reused. We need to ensure all threads are done with `BlockStore` before any thread starts `BlockLoad`. A `__syncthreads()` after `BlockStore` is necessary. So the loop body: ```cpp for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; ++tile) { ... BlockLoad(temp_storage.load).Load(in_it, thread_data, valid_items, 0.0f); __syncthreads(); float aggregate; BlockScan(temp_storage.scan).InclusiveSum(thread_data, thread_data, aggregate); #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ITEMS_PER_THREAD; ++i) { thread_data[i] += carry; } __syncthreads(); ReverseIterator out_it{row_out + cols - 1 - offset_r}; BlockStore(temp_storage.store).Store(out_it, thread_data, valid_items); __syncthreads(); carry += aggregate; } ``` This looks correct. One concern: `BlockLoad` with `BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE` and a custom iterator. Does CUB's `BlockLoad` support arbitrary iterators? Now, let's construct the Python source. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cub/cub.cuh> struct ConstReverseIterator { const float* ptr; __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator operator+(int offset) const { return ConstReverseIterator{ptr - offset}; } __device__ __forceinline__ float operator[](int offset) const { return ptr[-offset]; } }; struct ReverseIterator { float* ptr; __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator operator+(int offset) const { return ReverseIterator{ptr - offset}; } __device__ __forceinline__ float& operator[](int offset) const { return ptr[-offset]; } }; template <int BLOCK_THREADS, int ITEMS_PER_THREAD> __global__ void reverse_cumsum_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int rows, int cols) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= rows) return; const float* row_in = input + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols; float* row_out = output + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols; using BlockLoad = cub::BlockLoad<float, BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD, cub::BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE>; using BlockStore = cub::BlockStore<float, BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD, cub::BLOCK_STORE_WARP_TRANSPOSE>; using BlockScan = cub::BlockScan<float, BLOCK_THREADS>; union TempStorage { typename BlockLoad::TempStorage load; typename BlockStore::TempStorage store; typename BlockScan::TempStorage scan; }; __shared__ TempStorage temp_storage; float thread_data[ITEMS_PER_THREAD]; const int tile_size = BLOCK_THREADS * ITEMS_PER_THREAD; int num_tiles = (cols + tile_size - 1) / tile_size; float carry = 0.0f; for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; ++tile) { int offset_r = tile * tile_size; int valid_items = min(tile_size, cols - offset_r); ConstReverseIterator in_it{row_in + cols - 1 - offset_r}; BlockLoad(temp_storage.load).Load(in_it, thread_data, valid_items, 0.0f); __syncthreads(); float aggregate = 0.0f; BlockScan(temp_storage.scan).InclusiveSum(thread_data, thread_data, aggregate); #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ITEMS_PER_THREAD; ++i) { thread_data[i] += carry; } __syncthreads(); ReverseIterator out_it{row_out + cols - 1 - offset_r}; BlockStore(temp_storage.store).Store(out_it, thread_data, valid_items); __syncthreads(); carry += aggregate; } } torch::Tensor reverse_cumsum_cuda(torch::Tensor input, int64_t dim) { auto x = input.contiguous(); int64_t ndim = x.dim(); if (dim < 0) dim += ndim; // If target dimension is not the last one, permute so that it is contiguous. if (dim != ndim - 1) { std::vector<int64_t> perm(ndim); for (int64_t i = 0; i < ndim; ++i) perm[i] = i; perm.erase(perm.begin() + dim); perm.push_back(dim); auto y = x.permute(perm).contiguous(); auto out_y = torch::empty_like(y); int64_t cols = y.size(-1);
