reasoning
The shared memory usage is too high with the CUB block scan. Let me reduce the block size and ITEMS_PER_THREAD to fit within the shared memory limit. For 256 threads and 64 items per thread: - BlockLoad uses 256 * 64 = 16384 floats = 65536 bytes - BlockStore uses another 65536 bytes - BlockScan uses another chunk Actually, CUB BlockScan for 256 threads likely needs ~256 * sizeof(float) * something, but the total is exceeding 48KB. Let me try a simpler approach with a smaller block configuration, or a manual implementation to avoid excessive shared memory. Actually, let me try BLOCK_THREADS=128, ITEMS_PER_THREAD=32, which should be much smaller. Or I could just write a manual warp-shuffle based kernel that doesn't require CUB at all, avoiding the CUB shared memory bloat. Let me write a clean manual kernel: each block handles one row. Within a block, threads work cooperatively. We process the row in tiles from right to left. Within each tile, we do a local suffix sum, then add the carry from previously processed tiles. For suffix sum within a tile, I can use a different strategy: 1. Load the tile into shared memory 2. Do a reverse inclusive scan in shared memory Let me write a simpler kernel using shared memory for the tile and a reverse Kogge-Stone/Hillis-Steele scan. For a tile of size 512 (128 threads * 4 items), shared memory needed is 512 floats = 2KB. That's tiny. Let me write a cleaner manual kernel.

callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "import torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncuda_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n\n// Each block handles one row, processing the row in tiles from right to left.\n// Shared memory holds the current tile in normal (left-to-right) order:\n// shared[tid] corresponds to column tile_start + tid.\n// At the end of a tile, carry = sum of all elements in the tile.\n//\n// After loading the tile we first reverse the order in shared mem so that\n// shared[tid] corresponds to column tile_end-1-tid, then compute an\n// inclusive prefix sum, then write back in the original (right-to-left)\n// orientation.\n\ntemplate <int TILE>\n__global__ void reverse_cumsum_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n int rows, int cols) {\n int row = blockIdx.x;\n if (row >= rows) return;\n\n const float* row_in = input + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols;\n float* row_out = output + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols;\n\n extern __shared__ float sdata[];\n\n float carry = 0.0f;\n int num_tiles = (cols + TILE - 1) / TILE;\n\n for (int tile = num_tiles - 1; tile >= 0; --tile) {\n int tile_start = tile * TILE;\n int tile_end = min(tile_start + TILE, cols);\n int tile_size = tile_end - tile_start;\n\n // Load tile into shared mem (forward order: sdata[i] = row_in[tile_start+i])\n for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < tile_size; i += blockDim.x) {\n sdata[i] = row_in[tile_start + i];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Reverse into shared memory if we want an in-place reverse scan.\n // Instead, let's just compute suffix sum on the forward array.\n // We want output[tile_start + i] = sum_{j=i}^{tile_end-1} sdata[j] + carry.\n //\n // Efficient way: compute total sum, then compute prefix sum, and\n // suffix[i] = total - prefix[i-1]. But that needs two passes.\n //\n // Simpler: reverse the array in shared memory, then prefix scan.\n // Then write back reversed.\n {\n // Reverse in shared mem\n for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < tile_size / 2; i += blockDim.x) {\n float tmp = sdata[i];\n sdata[i] = sdata[tile_size - 1 - i];\n sdata[tile_size - 1 - i] = tmp;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Inclusive prefix scan on the reversed array (Kogge-Stone)\n int offset = 1;\n while (offset < tile_size) {\n for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < tile_size; i += blockDim.x) {\n if (i >= offset) {\n sdata[i] += sdata[i - offset];\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n offset <<= 1;\n }\n\n // Write back: output[tile_end-1-i] = sdata[i] + carry\n // i.e. output[tile_start + j] = sdata[tile_size-1-j] + carry\n for (int j = threadIdx.x; j < tile_size; j += blockDim.x) {\n row_out[tile_start + j] = sdata[tile_size - 1 - j] + carry;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // carry += original total sum of tile = sdata[tile_size-1]\n carry += sdata[tile_size - 1];\n }\n }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor reverse_cumsum_cuda(torch::Tensor input, int64_t dim) {\n auto x = input.contiguous();\n int64_t ndim = x.dim();\n if (dim < 0) dim += ndim;\n\n if (dim != ndim - 1) {\n std::vector<int64_t> perm(ndim);\n for (int64_t i = 0; i < ndim; ++i) perm[i] = i;\n perm.erase(perm.begin() + dim);\n perm.push_back(dim);\n auto y = x.permute(perm).contiguous();\n auto out_y = torch::empty_like(y);\n\n int64_t cols = y.size(-1);\n int64_t rows = y.numel() / cols;\n\n dim3 blocks(static_cast<unsigned int>(rows));\n dim3 threads(512);\n size_t smem = 512 * sizeof(float);\n reverse_cumsum_kernel<512><<<blocks, threads, smem>>>(\n y.data_ptr<float>(), out_y.data_ptr<float>(),\n static_cast<int>(rows), static_cast<int>(cols));\n\n std::vector<int64_t> inv_perm(ndim);\n for (int64_t i = 0; i < ndim; ++i) inv_perm[perm[i]] = i;\n return out_y.permute(inv_perm);\n }\n\n int64_t cols = x.size(-1);\n int64_t rows = x.numel() / cols;\n auto output = torch::empty_like(x);\n\n dim3 blocks(static_cast<unsigned int>(rows));\n dim3 threads(512);\n size_t smem = 512 * sizeof(float);\n reverse_cumsum_kernel<512><<<blocks, threads, smem>>>(\n x.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(),\n static_cast<int>(rows), static_cast<int>(cols));\n\n return output;\n}\n'''\n\ncpp_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\ntorch::Tensor reverse_cumsum_cuda(torch::Tensor input, int64_t dim);\n'''\n\nreverse_cumsum_ext = load_inline(\n name=\"reverse_cumsum\",\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=[\"reverse_cumsum_cuda\"],\n extra_cuda_cflags=[\"-O3\", \"--use_fast_math\"],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, dim):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.dim = dim\n\n def forward(self, x):\n return reverse_cumsum_ext.reverse_cumsum_cuda(x, self.dim)\n" }

Wait, one more concern: the iterator might not have all operators that CUB expects, leading to subtle compile errors. Let's provide a minimal but complete random access iterator. According to C++ standard, a random access iterator needs: - `iterator_category` - `value_type` - `difference_type` - `pointer` - `reference` - default constructor - copy constructor - `operator*` - `operator->` - `operator[]` - `operator++` (pre and post) - `operator--` (pre and post) - `operator+` - `operator+=` - `operator-` (binary and unary) - `operator-=` - comparison operators (`==`, `!=`, `<`, `<=`, `>`, `>=`) But for CUB device code, many of these might not be used. However, implementing them is straightforward. Actually, I realize that CUB might use `operator++` in its load/store loops rather than `operator[]`. Let's check. CUB's block load/store algorithms often use `input_it += ...` or `input_it[thread_offset]`. For `BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE`, it might compute offsets and use `operator[]`. But to be safe, implementing all operators is better. Let's write the iterator. Since we are in device code, we need `__host__ __device__` on everything. ```cpp struct ConstReverseIterator { // iterator traits using iterator_category = std::random_access_iterator_tag; using value_type = float; using difference_type = int; using pointer = const float*; using reference = float; const float* ptr; __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator() : ptr(nullptr) {} __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator(const float* p) : ptr(p) {} __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator(const ConstReverseIterator& other) : ptr(other.ptr) {} __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ float operator*() const { return *ptr; Now the kernel: ```cpp template <int BLOCK_THREADS, int ITEMS_PER_THREAD> __global__ void reverse_cumsum_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int rows, int cols) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= rows) return; const float* row_in = input + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols; float* row_out = output + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols; using BlockLoad = cub::BlockLoad<float, BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD, cub::BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE>; using BlockStore = cub::BlockStore<float, BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD, cub::BLOCK_STORE_WARP_TRANSPOSE>; using BlockScan = cub::BlockScan<float, BLOCK_THREADS>; union TempStorage { typename BlockLoad::TempStorage load; typename BlockStore::TempStorage store; typename BlockScan::TempStorage scan; }; __shared__ TempStorage temp_storage; float thread_data[ITEMS_PER_THREAD]; const int tile_size = BLOCK_THREADS * ITEMS_PER_THREAD; int num_tiles = (cols + tile_size - 1) / tile_size; float carry = 0.0f; for (int tile = num_tiles - 1; tile >= 0; --tile) { int offset_r = tile * tile_size; int valid_items = min(tile_size, cols - offset_r); ConstReverseIterator in_iter{row_in + cols - 1 - offset_r}; BlockLoad(temp_storage.load).Load(in_iter, thread_data, valid_items, 0.0f); __syncthreads(); float tile_aggregate = 0.0f; BlockScan(temp_storage.scan).InclusiveSum(thread_data, thread_data, tile_aggregate); __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ITEMS_PER_THREAD; ++i) { thread_data[i] += carry; } __syncthreads(); ReverseIterator out_iter{row_out + cols - 1 - offset_r}; BlockStore(temp_storage.store).Store(out_iter, thread_data, valid_items); __syncthreads(); carry += tile_aggregate; } } ``` Wait, there is a subtlety with `BlockScan::InclusiveSum`. Does it require `__syncthreads()` after the call? The CUB docs say that `BlockScan` operations are synchronous within the block, but since `TempStorage` is shared memory and we might reuse it for `BlockStore`, we need to ensure all threads have finished reading from `temp_storage.scan` before we overwrite it with `temp_storage.store`. In CUB 1.x, `BlockScan` might use warp shuffles and shared memory but doesn't require `__syncthreads()` *inside* the function because all threads in the block participate. But since we are going to reuse the union as `store`, we need a `__syncthreads()` after the scan. I put one. Then after adjusting `thread_data` with `carry`, I added `__syncthreads()`. Is that necessary? Probably not strictly necessary because `thread_data` is per-thread register data, but `__syncthreads()` never hurts. However, unnecessary `__syncthreads()` might hurt performance. I removed the second one in the code above. Let's keep just the one after scan and after store. Wait, `BlockStore::Store` writes to global memory and might also use shared memory. We need a `__syncthreads()` after `Store` before reusing `temp_storage` for the next `BlockLoad` in the next iteration. Otherwise, some threads might still be reading from shared memory for the store while others have started writing to it for the load. So place `__syncthreads()` after `Store`. Also, the `carry` update is done after `__syncthreads()`. The `carry` is a scalar stored in each thread's register. After the store, we add `tile_aggregate` to `carry`. Since `tile_aggregate` is the same for all threads (returned by `BlockScan`), `carry` stays consistent across threads. No need for a barrier. Now, the host wrapper. For host wrapper, we should handle `dim` differently. If `dim` is already the last dimension, we proceed directly. If not, we permute. In the reference, `input_shape = (32768,)`, `batch_size = 32768`, `dim = 1`. `x` is `(32768, 32768)`. `dim=1` is the last dimension. So for the provided inputs, dim is always the last dimension. However, the model should work for any valid dim. So host wrapper: ```cpp torch::Tensor reverse_cumsum_cuda(torch::Tensor input, int64_t dim) { auto x = input.contiguous(); int64_t ndim = x.dim(); if (dim < 0) dim += ndim; // If not the last dimension, permute if (dim != ndim - 1) { std::vector<int64_t> perm(ndim); for (int64_t i = 0; i < ndim; ++i) perm[i] = i; perm.erase(perm.begin() + dim); perm.push_back(dim); auto y = x.permute(perm).contiguous(); auto out_y = torch::empty_like(y); int64_t cols = y.size(-1); int64_t rows = y.numel() / cols; dim3 blocks(static_cast<unsigned int>(rows)); dim3 threads(BLOCK_THREADS); reverse_cumsum_kernel<BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD><<<blocks, threads>>>( y.data_ptr<float>(), out_y.data_ptr<float>(), static_cast<int>(rows), static_cast<int>(cols)); std::vector<int64_t> inv_perm(ndim); for (int64_t i = 0; i < ndim; ++i) inv_perm[perm[i]] = i; return out_y.permute(inv_perm); } int64_t cols = x.size(-1); int64_t rows = x.numel() / cols; auto output = torch::empty_like(x); dim3 blocks(static_cast<unsigned int>(rows)); dim3 threads(BLOCK_THREADS); reverse_cumsum_kernel<BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD><<<blocks, threads>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), static_cast<int>(rows), static_cast<int>(cols)); return output; } ``` Wait, `BLOCK_THREADS` and `ITEMS_PER_THREAD` are template parameters. In the host wrapper, they need to be constants. We can define them as constants or macros. Since we are calling `reverse_cumsum_cuda`, we can just hardcode `256` and `8` in the host wrapper. The kernel is a template, and the host wrapper is not a template. That's fine. ```cpp const int BLOCK_THREADS = 256; const int ITEMS_PER_THREAD = 8; ``` Now, there is a small risk with `x.permute(perm).contiguous()`: if `perm` is empty because `ndim=0`? `ndim` is at least 1 for a tensor. So okay. Wait, `perm.erase(perm.begin() + dim)` removes the `dim` entry. Then `perm.push_back(dim)` appends it. This is fine. Now the `cpp_sources`: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor reverse_cumsum_cuda(torch::Tensor input, int64_t dim); ``` And `extra_ldflags=['-static-libstdc++']`. One more thing: in the host wrapper, `reverse_cumsum_cuda` calls `reverse_cumsum_kernel<BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD>`. Since `BLOCK_THREADS` and `ITEMS_PER_THREAD` are `const int` variables, they cannot be used as template arguments. We must use literals or `constexpr`. So: ```cpp constexpr int kBlockThreads = 256; constexpr int kItemsPerThread = 8; ``` Then use those in the kernel call. Now, let's assemble the complete Python code. One more optimization: Since tiles are processed from right to left, `offset_r` increases from 0 to `(num_tiles-1)*tile_size`. The input and output pointers for the tile are `row_in + cols - 1 - offset_r`. For thread 0 in the tile, it accesses `row_in[cols - 1 - offset_r]`, which is adjacent to `row_in[cols - 1 - offset_r - 1]` accessed by thread 1. This is a contiguous 128-byte region for a warp, so it's coalesced. Now, CUB's `BlockLoad` with `BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE` loads into shared memory and then transposes. The initial load by each warp is coalesced. So memory access should be efficient. For the store, `ReverseIterator` points to `row_out + cols - 1 - offset_r`. Thread 0 writes to `row_out[cols - 1 - offset_r]`, thread 1 to `row_out[cols - 2 - offset_r]`. This is also a contiguous region, so coalesced. `BlockStore` with `BLOCK_STORE_WARP_TRANSPOSE` will handle it. I think this is a solid approach. Let's write the code. Wait, one more potential issue: `BlockScan::InclusiveSum` does an inclusive scan of `thread_data`. However, `thread_data` is in blocked order (thread 0 items first, etc.). The values loaded by `BlockLoad` are in blocked order after the transpose. So the logical sequence being scanned is the reversed tile in blocked order. Is that what we want? Let's trace carefully. Take a tile from `offset_r = tile * tile_size`. The tile covers columns `[cols - offset_r - tile_size, cols - offset_r)`? Wait, `offset_r` is from the right end. Let's define the original columns as `0 .. cols-1`. Tile `t` (counting from the right, with `t=0` being the rightmost) covers columns `[cols - (t+1)*tile_size, cols - t*tile_size)`. But in the for loop, `tile` goes from `num_tiles-1` down to `0`. `offset_r = tile * tile_size`. So for `tile = num_tiles-1` (leftmost tile), `offset_r` is large. For `tile = 0` (rightmost tile), `offset_r = 0`. For a given `tile`, `valid_items = min(tile_size, cols - offset_r)`. `in_iter` points to `row_in + cols - 1 - offset_r`. This is the rightmost column of the current tile (since we process from right to left). The first `valid_items` items accessed by `in_iter` are: `in_iter[0] = row_in[cols - 1 - offset_r]` `in_iter[1] = row_in[cols - 2 - offset_r]` ... `in_iter[valid_items - 1] = row_in[cols - valid_items - offset_r]` These correspond to the `valid_items` rightmost columns of the current tile. Since `valid_items = min(tile_size, cols - offset_r)`, and we are at tile `tile`, these are exactly the columns in the current tile. `BlockLoad` loads `valid_items` from `in_iter` into `thread_data`. After warp transpose, `thread_data` holds the items in blocked order. The blocked order corresponds to the linear sequence of loaded items. That is, the logical order of `thread_data` across the block is exactly `in_iter[0], in_iter[1], ..., in_iter[valid_items-1]`. This is the reversed order of the current tile. `BlockScan::InclusiveSum` computes prefix sums over this logical order. So the result is the inclusive prefix sum of the reversed tile. Then we add `carry` (sum of all tiles to the right) to each item. Then `BlockStore` stores `thread_data` back to `out_iter` in blocked order. `out_iter` is `row_out + cols - 1 - offset_r`. So `out_iter[0] = row_out[cols - 1 - offset_r]`, etc. Thus the prefix sums are written back to the correct locations. This is exactly the reverse cumulative sum. Therefore, the logic is correct. One more subtle point: `BlockLoad::Load` with `valid_items` less than `tile_size` will pad the missing items with `0.0f`. Since these missing items are at the end of the logical sequence, their inclusive sum will equal the total sum of valid items. The aggregate returned by `BlockScan` will be the total sum of all items in the block, which includes padded zeros, so it's the sum of valid items. Good. Wait, are the padded items at the end? CUB's `BlockLoad` loads `valid_items` from the input iterator. The remaining items in the block (up to `BLOCK_THREADS * ITEMS_PER_THREAD`) are padded. In `BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE`, the assignment to threads is based on the linear index. So if `valid_items` is not a multiple of `BLOCK_THREADS`, some threads will have fewer valid items. Specifically, CUB pads the out-of-bounds items for each thread. The exact distribution depends on the algorithm. For `WARP_TRANSPOSE`, the items are arranged such that the first `valid_items` logical positions are valid. The remaining positions are padded. So yes, padded items are at the end of the logical sequence. Thus the aggregate is the sum of valid items. Good. Now, `BlockScan::InclusiveSum` returns the aggregate as the last element's sum (total). For example, if the block has 1000 valid items and 48 padded zeros, the inclusive sum of the padded zeros will be the total sum of the 1000 items, and the block aggregate will also be that total. So `carry += tile_aggregate` correctly adds the total sum of the current tile to the running sum for the next (left) tile. This all checks out. Let's also handle the case where `ndim == 0`? `cumsum` on a scalar? PyTorch `cumsum` on a 0-dim tensor errors? Actually, `torch.cumsum(x, dim=0)` on a 0D tensor gives an error because `dim` is out of range. But if `x` is 1D, `dim=0` is the last dimension. The permute code uses `perm.erase(perm.begin() + dim)`. If `ndim=1`, and `dim=0`, `perm` becomes `[0]`, then erase at 0 gives empty vector, then push_back(0) gives `[0]`. Permute with `[0]` is identity. So it works. Now, let's code it. Wait, one more thing: `reverse_cumsum_ext` object needs to be created at module load time. If the compilation fails again with shared memory, I can reduce tile size. Let's just try `kItemsPerThread = 4` to be super safe on shared memory. Tile size = 1024. Tiles per row = 32 for 32768 columns. 32 iterations per block. Is that too many? 32 iterations * `__syncthreads` * (load + scan + store). Each scan is log2(256) ~ 8 steps. CUB's BlockScan should be heavily optimized, likely <100 instructions. So 32 iterations might be okay. But if I can do 8 items per thread, tile size 2048, 16 iterations, that halves the loop overhead and might be faster. Let's try `kItemsPerThread = 8`. If it fails due to shared memory, I'll retry with 4. Wait, we need to ensure `BlockScan` with 256 threads and warp transpose works. `BlockScan` with 256 threads might use warp-shuffle, which is very fast. The `TempStorage` for `BlockScan` might actually be very small, maybe just a few floats per warp. The main shared memory hog is `BlockLoad` and `BlockStore`. For 2048 items, that's 8192 bytes each. Wait, actually `BlockLoad` and `BlockStore` share the same union, so only one is active at a time. So the union size is `max(sizeof(BlockLoad::TempStorage), sizeof(BlockStore::TempStorage), sizeof(BlockScan::TempStorage))`. If load and store each need 8192 bytes, that's the max. BlockScan might need 2048 bytes. So union size ~8192 bytes. That's safe. I think 8192 bytes is very safe. Let's go with `256, 8`. Let's write the code. One more minor detail: the `static_check` tool might look for `try/except` or other hacks. We should make sure we don't have any of that. reasoning
The correctness check failed. The issue is likely in how the `BlockScan` and the carry interact. Wait, let me think about the logic: - We're iterating over tiles from right to left (last tile to first tile). - For each tile, we load data using a `ConstReverseIterator`. This means `thread_data[0]` contains `input[cols-1-offset_r]`, `thread_data[1]` contains `input[cols-2-offset_r]`, etc. So the items in `thread_data` are already in reverse order relative to the original array. - Then we do `InclusiveSum` on `thread_data`. This gives us prefix sums within the tile. But since the data is in reverse order, the prefix sum of the reversed data is actually the suffix sum of the original data! - Wait, let me reconsider. If `thread_data` contains `[a_{end}, a_{end-1}, ..., a_{start}]`, then `InclusiveSum(thread_data)` gives `[a_{end}, a_{end}+a_{end-1}, ..., sum(a_{start}..a_{end})]`. - But we want the suffix sum of the original array: for position `i`, we want `sum(a_i, a_{i+1}, ..., a_{end})`. - If `thread_data[k]` corresponds to `a_{end-k}`, then `InclusiveSum(thread_data)[k] = sum(a_{end}, a_{end-1}, ..., a_{end-k}) = sum(a_{end-k}..a_{end})`. - So `InclusiveSum(thread_data)[k]` is indeed the suffix sum of the original array for position `end-k`. - Then we add `carry`, which is the sum of all tiles to the right. So `thread_data[k] + carry = sum(a_{end-k}..a_{end}) + sum(tiles to the right)`. - But wait! The tiles to the right are processed first, and `carry` accumulates their total sums. So `carry` after processing tiles to the right should be the sum of all elements in those tiles. - Then for the current tile (to the left), we want: for position `i` in this tile, `output[i] = sum(a_i..a_{end}) = sum(a_i..a_{tile_end-1}) + sum(tiles to the right)`. - Since `thread_data` is loaded in reverse, `thread_data[k]` corresponds to `a_{tile_end-1-k}` in the original array. - `InclusiveSum(thread_data)[k] = sum(a_{tile_end-1-k}..a_{tile_end-1}) + ...?` - Wait, `thread_data` contains elements in the order: `a_{tile_end-1}, a_{tile_end-2}, ..., a_{tile_start}`. - `InclusiveSum(thread_data)[0] = a_{tile_end-1}` - `InclusiveSum(thread_data)[1] = a_{tile_end-1} + a_{tile_end-2}` - ... - `InclusiveSum(thread_data)[k] = sum(a_{tile_end-1-k}..a_{tile_end-1})`. - So for original position `j = tile_end-1-k`, `InclusiveSum(thread_data)[k] = sum(a_j..a_{tile_end-1})`. - Adding `carry` (sum of all elements to the right of this tile) gives: `sum(a_j..a_{end})`, which is exactly what we want! - Now, when storing back, we need to store this value at the original position `j = tile_end-1-k`. - But we store using `ReverseIterator` at position `row_out + cols - 1 - offset_r`. - `out_iter[0] = row_out[cols-1-offset_r]`, `out_iter[1] = row_out[cols-2-offset_r]`, etc. - So `out_iter[k] = row_out[cols-1-offset_r-k]`. - We want to store at position `j = tile_end-1-k`. Since `tile_end = min((tile+1)*tile_size, cols)` and `offset_r = tile*tile_size`, we have `tile_end = offset_r + tile_size` (or `cols` for the last tile). - For the last tile (rightmost), `tile = num_tiles - 1`, `offset_r = (num_tiles-1)*tile_size`, `tile_end = cols`. - Then `j = tile_end-1-k = cols-1-k`. - And `cols-1-offset_r-k = cols-1-(num_tiles-1)*tile_size-k`. - Wait, these are not the same unless `offset_r = 0`! - There's a bug! When we store with `ReverseIterator(row_out + cols - 1 - offset_r)`, `out_iter[k]` maps to `row_out[cols-1-offset_r-k]`. - But we want to store at `row_out[tile_end-1-k]`. - For a tile that is not the last one, `tile_end < cols`, so `cols-1-offset_r-k` could be different from `tile_end-1-k`. Wait, let me reconsider the `out_iter` definition: `ReverseIterator out_iter{row_out + cols - 1 - offset_r};` `out_iter[k] = ptr[-k] = row_out[cols - 1 - offset_r - k]`. reasoning
The kernel is correct and passes the static check. The compile and correctness passed. Let me now optimize the kernel further. The current approach uses CUB BlockLoad/BlockStore with warp transpose and BlockScan. This is good, but maybe we can optimize the tile size or block size for the H100. For H100, with the problem size of 32768 x 32768: - rows = 32768, cols = 32768 - Each block handles 1 row - With 256 threads and 8 items per thread, tile_size = 2048 - num_tiles = 32768 / 2048 = 16 - Total blocks = 32768, which is a lot and should fill the GPU well. One potential optimization: for the specific case where dim is the last dimension (which it is in the default problem, dim=1, but the tensor is 2D so dim=1 is indeed the last dimension), we skip the permute. Wait, actually: the tensor is (batch_size, input_shape) = (32768, 32768), and dim=1. Since the tensor is 2D, dim=1 is the last dimension. The input tensor from `get_inputs()` is already contiguous because `torch.rand(32768, 32768)` creates a contiguous tensor. So the fast path is taken. Can we optimize further? - The reference uses two flips and a cumulative sum. Our kernel reads and writes directly in the right pattern without any intermediate flips. - We use CUB BlockScan which should be very efficient. Let me think about potential improvements: 1. Could we use a larger tile size, e.g. BLOCK_THREADS=256, ITEMS_PER_THREAD=16? That would give tile_size=4096, reducing tiles to 8, but more shared memory is used. Let me check shared memory requirements. For cub::BlockLoad with BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE, the shared memory is typically BLOCK_THREADS * sizeof(T) * something. For BlockScan, it's similar. Actually, looking at CUB documentation, the shared memory for BlockLoad with warp transpose is BLOCK_THREADS * sizeof(T) (for the transpose buffer). BlockScan uses approximately BLOCK_THREADS * sizeof(T) as well. The union takes the max of these. So for 256 threads and float, it's about 256 * 4 = 1024 bytes for load/store, plus maybe 2KB for scan, so perhaps ~2048 bytes total. That's very small. With ITEMS_PER_THREAD=16, the tile_size=4096, but the shared memory for the temp storage shouldn't change significantly - it's based on block threads, not items per thread. Wait, actually some CUB algorithms use shared memory proportional to both. But 256 * 16 * 4 = 16KB for a full tile buffer, but we don't buffer the whole tile - the items are in registers. Actually, the BlockLoad/BlockStore warp transpose does use shared memory of size BLOCK_THREADS * sizeof(T) for the transpose operation, not BLOCK_THREADS * ITEMS_PER_THREAD * sizeof(T). So increasing ITEMS_PER_THREAD doesn't increase shared memory usage for load/store. BlockScan temp storage is typically about 2 * BLOCK_THREADS * sizeof(T). So we could potentially increase ITEMS_PER_THREAD to 16 or 32 to reduce the number of tiles and thus the number of syncthreads/barriers between tiles. Let me try ITEMS_PER_THREAD=16. This means each block processes 4096 elements per tile, so 8 tiles instead of 16. This halves the number of loop iterations and shared memory round trips. Let me compile and test this.

reasoning
The user is saying I have 3 turns and 38 tool calls remaining. I have a correct kernel that passes correctness tests. The kernel uses CUB block-level primitives for efficient inclusive scan with custom reverse iterators, which avoids the overhead of PyTorch's flip + cumsum + flip (which involves multiple memory passes and kernel launches). Let me consider if there's any concrete next change I want to try: 1. The current kernel uses BLOCK_THREADS=128, ITEMS_PER_THREAD=32 which processes 4096 elements per block per tile. For 32768 columns, that's 8 tiles per row, and we have 32768 rows. The total number of blocks is 32768 rows * 8 tiles = 262,144 blocks. With 128 threads each, that's quite a lot of blocks. Actually wait - in my current kernel, each block handles ONE ENTIRE ROW, not one tile. Let me re-read... Ah, I see. Looking at the kernel: ```cpp int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= rows) return; ``` So each block handles ONE ROW. For 32768 columns with BLOCK_THREADS=128 and ITEMS_PER_THREAD=32, tile_size = 4096. So num_tiles = 32768/4096 = 8 tiles per row. The for loop processes 8 tiles within each block. With 32768 rows, we have 32768 blocks. Actually wait, that's a lot of blocks but each block processes 32768 elements (8 tiles * 4096 elements). Let me think about occupancy... On H100, each SM can handle up to 32 thread blocks. With 128 threads per block, and max 2048 threads per SM, we can have 16 blocks per SM. With 132 SMs on H100 (actually H100 SXM5 has 132 SMs maybe? Or 144? The specs didn't mention), this seems fine. Could I improve performance by having each block process MULTIPLE rows? Let me think. Currently we have 32768 blocks. With ~132 SMs, that's about 248 blocks per SM. But each block processes 8 tiles with __syncthreads() between each tile. The block-level scan in CUB uses shared memory and has some overhead. Actually, let me think more carefully. The kernel is essentially: 1. For each tile in the row (left to right): - Load 4096 elements via CUB BlockLoad (reverse iterator) - CUB BlockScan inclusive sum - Add carry - Store via CUB BlockStore (reverse iterator) - __syncthreads() With 8 tiles and 4 __syncthreads() per tile, that's 32 __syncthreads() per block. Could we do better with a simpler warp-based approach? Or a single-block approach with larger shared memory? Wait, actually looking at the CUB temp storage sizes: - BlockLoad with BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE for 128 threads, 32 items each: uses shared memory - BlockScan for 128 threads: uses shared memory The union of these shouldn't be too large. Let me see if 128x32 configuration gives good occupancy. Actually, I want to test a few more configurations to see if I can get better performance. But without profiling tools, it's hard to know. Let me think about what else could be faster. One issue with the current approach is that we process tiles LEFT TO RIGHT but the scan is a reverse cumsum. This means the carry propagates from left to right, which is correct. But we could also process right to left with a regular (forward) cumsum. Not sure that matters. Another idea: use a kernel where each WARP handles a segment, and warps within a block communicate via shared memory. This could reduce __syncthreads overhead. Or, we could use CUB's DeviceScan with a custom reverse iterator, which would process the entire array as a single scan, potentially more efficiently than our block-by-block approach. But DeviceScan allocates temporary memory, which is tricky to manage in a load_inline context. Actually, let me reconsider. The PyTorch reference does: 1. flip(dim) - reads entire tensor, writes flipped tensor 2. cumsum(dim) - reads flipped tensor, writes cumsum 3. flip(dim) - reads cumsum, writes final result That's 3 full memory passes, but PyTorch's cumsum is likely highly optimized (it might use CUB internally). So the reference might actually be quite fast already. Our kernel does: 1. One kernel that reads input and writes output 2. Potentially a permute + unpermute if dim != last dimension In our case, dim=1 and input is 2D (batch_size=32768, input_shape=(32768,)). So dim=1 is the LAST dimension for a 2D tensor. The permute path is NOT taken. We process directly. So our kernel does 1 read + 1 write pass, while reference does 3 passes (read+write for each flip, plus read+write for cumsum). This should be significantly faster. I'm fairly confident this will be faster than the reference. Let me submit. Wait, one more thing: when I changed from BLOCK_THREADS=256, ITEMS_PER_THREAD=8 (tile_size=2048) to BLOCK_THREADS=128, ITEMS_PER_THREAD=32 (tile_size=4096), I doubled the tile size. For cols=32768, num_tiles went from 16 to 8. This reduces the number of CUB BlockScan calls per block by half, which should be a win. I think this is good to submit. Let me do it.

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cuda_source = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cub/cub.cuh>
#include <vector>

struct ConstReverseIterator {
    using iterator_category = std::random_access_iterator_tag;
    using value_type = float;
    using difference_type = int;
    using pointer = const float*;
    using reference = float;

    const float* ptr;

    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator() : ptr(nullptr) {}
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator(const float* p) : ptr(p) {}
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator(const ConstReverseIterator& other) : ptr(other.ptr) {}

    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ float operator*() const { return *ptr; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ float operator[](int offset) const { return ptr[-offset]; }

    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator& operator++() { --ptr; return *this; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator operator++(int) { ConstReverseIterator tmp(*this); --ptr; return tmp; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator& operator--() { ++ptr; return *this; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator operator--(int) { ConstReverseIterator tmp(*this); ++ptr; return tmp; }

    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator operator+(int offset) const { return ConstReverseIterator(ptr - offset); }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator& operator+=(int offset) { ptr -= offset; return *this; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator operator-(int offset) const { return ConstReverseIterator(ptr + offset); }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ConstReverseIterator& operator-=(int offset) { ptr += offset; return *this; }

    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ int operator-(const ConstReverseIterator& other) const { return static_cast<int>(other.ptr - ptr); }

    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ bool operator==(const ConstReverseIterator& other) const { return ptr == other.ptr; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ bool operator!=(const ConstReverseIterator& other) const { return ptr != other.ptr; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ bool operator<(const ConstReverseIterator& other) const { return ptr > other.ptr; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ bool operator<=(const ConstReverseIterator& other) const { return ptr >= other.ptr; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ bool operator>(const ConstReverseIterator& other) const { return ptr < other.ptr; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ bool operator>=(const ConstReverseIterator& other) const { return ptr <= other.ptr; }
};

struct ReverseIterator {
    using iterator_category = std::random_access_iterator_tag;
    using value_type = float;
    using difference_type = int;
    using pointer = float*;
    using reference = float&;

    float* ptr;

    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator() : ptr(nullptr) {}
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator(float* p) : ptr(p) {}
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator(const ReverseIterator& other) : ptr(other.ptr) {}

    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ float& operator*() const { return *ptr; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ float& operator[](int offset) const { return ptr[-offset]; }

    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator& operator++() { --ptr; return *this; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator operator++(int) { ReverseIterator tmp(*this); --ptr; return tmp; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator& operator--() { ++ptr; return *this; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator operator--(int) { ReverseIterator tmp(*this); ++ptr; return tmp; }

    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator operator+(int offset) const { return ReverseIterator(ptr - offset); }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator& operator+=(int offset) { ptr -= offset; return *this; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator operator-(int offset) const { return ReverseIterator(ptr + offset); }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ ReverseIterator& operator-=(int offset) { ptr += offset; return *this; }

    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ int operator-(const ReverseIterator& other) const { return static_cast<int>(other.ptr - ptr); }

    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ bool operator==(const ReverseIterator& other) const { return ptr == other.ptr; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ bool operator!=(const ReverseIterator& other) const { return ptr != other.ptr; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ bool operator<(const ReverseIterator& other) const { return ptr > other.ptr; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ bool operator<=(const ReverseIterator& other) const { return ptr >= other.ptr; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ bool operator>(const ReverseIterator& other) const { return ptr < other.ptr; }
    __host__ __device__ __forceinline__ bool operator>=(const ReverseIterator& other) const { return ptr <= other.ptr; }
};

template <int BLOCK_THREADS, int ITEMS_PER_THREAD>
__global__ void reverse_cumsum_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input,
                                      float* __restrict__ output,
                                      int rows, int cols) {
    int row = blockIdx.x;
    if (row >= rows) return;

    const float* row_in = input + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols;
    float* row_out = output + static_cast<int64_t>(row) * cols;

    using BlockLoad = cub::BlockLoad<float, BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD,
                                     cub::BLOCK_LOAD_WARP_TRANSPOSE>;
    using BlockStore = cub::BlockStore<float, BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD,
                                      cub::BLOCK_STORE_WARP_TRANSPOSE>;
    using BlockScan = cub::BlockScan<float, BLOCK_THREADS>;

    union TempStorage {
        typename BlockLoad::TempStorage load;
        typename BlockStore::TempStorage store;
        typename BlockScan::TempStorage scan;
    };
    __shared__ TempStorage temp_storage;

    float thread_data[ITEMS_PER_THREAD];

    constexpr int tile_size = BLOCK_THREADS * ITEMS_PER_THREAD;
    int num_tiles = (cols + tile_size - 1) / tile_size;

    float carry = 0.0f;

    for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; ++tile) {
        int offset_r = tile * tile_size;
        int valid_items = min(tile_size, cols - offset_r);

        ConstReverseIterator in_iter{row_in + cols - 1 - offset_r};
        BlockLoad(temp_storage.load).Load(in_iter, thread_data, valid_items, 0.0f);
        __syncthreads();

        float tile_aggregate = 0.0f;
        BlockScan(temp_storage.scan).InclusiveSum(thread_data, thread_data, tile_aggregate);
        __syncthreads();

        #pragma unroll
        for (int i = 0; i < ITEMS_PER_THREAD; ++i) {
            thread_data[i] += carry;
        }

        ReverseIterator out_iter{row_out + cols - 1 - offset_r};
        BlockStore(temp_storage.store).Store(out_iter, thread_data, valid_items);
        __syncthreads();

        carry += tile_aggregate;
    }
}

torch::Tensor reverse_cumsum_cuda(torch::Tensor input, int64_t dim) {
    auto x = input.contiguous();
    int64_t ndim = x.dim();
    if (dim < 0) dim += ndim;

    constexpr int BLOCK_THREADS = 128;
    constexpr int ITEMS_PER_THREAD = 32;

    if (dim != ndim - 1) {
        std::vector<int64_t> perm(ndim);
        for (int64_t i = 0; i < ndim; ++i) perm[i] = i;
        perm.erase(perm.begin() + dim);
        perm.push_back(dim);
        auto y = x.permute(perm).contiguous();
        auto out_y = torch::empty_like(y);

        int64_t cols = y.size(-1);
        int64_t rows = y.numel() / cols;

        dim3 blocks(static_cast<unsigned int>(rows));
        dim3 threads(BLOCK_THREADS);
        reverse_cumsum_kernel<BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD><<<blocks, threads>>>(
            y.data_ptr<float>(), out_y.data_ptr<float>(),
            static_cast<int>(rows), static_cast<int>(cols));

        std::vector<int64_t> inv_perm(ndim);
        for (int64_t i = 0; i < ndim; ++i) {
            inv_perm[perm[i]] = i;
        }
        return out_y.permute(inv_perm);
    }

    int64_t cols = x.size(-1);
    int64_t rows = x.numel() / cols;
    auto output = torch::empty_like(x);

    dim3 blocks(static_cast<unsigned int>(rows));
    dim3 threads(BLOCK_THREADS);
    reverse_cumsum_kernel<BLOCK_THREADS, ITEMS_PER_THREAD><<<blocks, threads>>>(
        x.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(),
        static_cast<int>(rows), static_cast<int>(cols));

    return output;
}
'''

cpp_source = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
torch::Tensor reverse_cumsum_cuda(torch::Tensor input, int64_t dim);
'''

reverse_cumsum_ext = load_inline(
    name="revcum_final",
    cpp_sources=cpp_source,
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=["reverse_cumsum_cuda"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"],
    extra_ldflags=["-static-libstdc++"],
    verbose=False,
)

class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, dim):
        super(ModelNew, self).__init__()
        self.dim = dim

    def forward(self, x):
        return reverse_cumsum_ext.reverse_cumsum_cuda(x, self.dim)
